The Ten Categories
intermediate
8 min
Defining Quantity and Its Place Among Categories
Author defines quantity and justifies its categorical position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why quantity is placed after substance in the categories and compare its generality with quality and relation.

Core Idea
Quantity is more general than quality and more established than relation, making it second only to substance.

The author begins by refining the definition of continuous quantity and then gives three reasons why quantity follows substance in the list of categories. Understanding these reasons helps you grasp the logical structure of the categories and how quantity relates to other fundamental concepts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of continuous quantity
Find where the author defines continuous quantity as 'that in which can be found that which counts something else'. Note the potential/actual distinction: magnitudes (potential) and numbers (actual).
The definition emphasizes the capacity to count other things, not just extension.
2
Extract the reasons for placement after substance
The author lists three reasons: participation in properties, generality over quality, and greater stability than relation. Locate each in the Arabic.
The phrase 'المشاركة في الخواص' refers to sharing properties with substance.
3
Compare quantity with quality
Notice the argument that number (from quantity) exists even in immaterial substances that lack quality, making quantity more general.
The author says العدد من الكمية وليس مقصورًا في وجوده على الأمور المقارنة للحركة والمادة.
4
Compare quantity with relation
The author states that relation is not settled (غير متقرر) in its subject as quantity is. Understand why this makes quantity more established.
Relation depends on two terms; quantity inheres in a single subject stably.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكمية المتصلة؛ بل الأولى أن يقال إنها عبارة عما يمكن أن يوجد فيه ما يعد غيره سواء كان ذلك بالقوة كما في المقادير، أو بالفعل كما في الأعداد. وإنها أي الكمية تذكر عقب الجوهر لمعان مستدعيه: منها المشاركة في الخواص؛ والمشاركة مما تقدم ذكره من قبل، ومنها أن الكمية أعم وجودًا من الكيفية وأصح وجودًا من المضاف. أما أنه أعم فلأن العدد من الكمية؛ وليس مقصورًا في وجوده على الأمور المقارنة للحركة والمادة دون المفارقة التي لا تقبل كيفية، ولا شيئًا خارجًا عن جوهرها. وأما أنها أصح فلأن المضاف غير متقرر في ذات موضوعه تقرر الكمية، وأيضًا فإن الكمية المتصلة توجد في جميع الأجسام الطبيعية من غير اختلاف، والكيفيات مختلفة فيها. والكمية إذا شاركت الكيفية في الجواهر فإنها تلزم الأول منها وهو الجسم، والكيفيات تلزم الجواهر النوعية السافلة والمتوسطة بعد الجسمية. ويمكن أن يقال من الوجوه ما عداها لكن الاشتغال بذلك اشتغال بما لا يغنيه؛ ولا يتم من المباحث فيها هو أنها جوهر أو عرض فإنها إذا كانت عبارة عن الجسمية التي تقارن المادة فبالحري أن تكون صورة مقومة. والصورة المقومة للجواهر جوهر لكن لا على المنطقي أن يتعرض لمثل هذا، أو يبينه على سبيل التحقيق؛ وقد كان بيانه في صناعة أخرى لكن يلزمه أن يقول إن الجسم لا يكون إلا وأن يكون متناهيا لكن حد الجسم من حيث هو جسم غير حد الجسم المتناهي. ولذلك قد يعقل الجسم دون التناهي ما لم يبرهن ببرهان يقرره؛ فالتناهي لا يكون داخلا في ماهية الجسم فالسطحي ليس جزءًا لحده. ومع ذلك فإنه وإن كان كل جسم متناهيًا فالأبعاد ليس بواجب حصولها في الجسم بالفعل فإن الكرة من حيث هي كرة جسم وليس تفرض فيها أبعاد متميزة بل الجسم إنما هو جسم؛ لأنه في طباعه بحيث يمكن أن تفرض فيه ثلاثة أبعاد متقاطعة على الإطلاق على حد واحد تقاطعًا على قوائم؛ وهذه هي الصورة الجسمية. فالجسم إذن هو الذي يمكن أن تفرض فيه الأبعاد الثلاثة المتقاطعة على زوايا قائمة والصورة الجسمية التي صورتها الجوهرية التي لا يزيد فيها جسم على جسم فهي صورة الجوهر أو جوهر بخلاف ما يقدر الأبعاد الثلاثة بقدر محدود أو غير محدود. فإن ذلك هو العرض الذي من باب الكمية والجسم الواحد قد يوجد بحيث يعرض له أن يختلف بحسب الكمية ولا يختلف بحسب الصورة مثل الشمعة التي يشكلها بشكل معين ثم يغيره إلى شكل بخلاف الأول. فنسبة الأبعاد في هذا الشكل مخالفة للنسبية في ذلك. وإن كانت الجسمية والشمعة باقية كما كانت فتكون الجسمية التي من باب الكمية تلزم الجسمية التي هي الصورة لما أن الجسم يلزمه التحدد وتكون الصورة الجسمية إذا جردت بكميتها أو جردت منها الكمية مأخوذة في الذهن يسمى المجرد جسما تعليمياً. وقد يعرف بأنه البعد القابل للتجزئة في ثلاث جهات متقاطعة على حد واحد تقاطعًا قائميًّا؛ وهو مجرد الطول والعرض والعمق في الحقيقة. ثم الكم ينقسم إلى متصل ومنفصل، وكذلك إلى ذي وضع وغير ذي وضع؛ وذو الوضع هو أن يكون لأجزائه مع الاتصال بقرر يمكن أن يشار إليه إشارة حسية، ويسمى عظما ومقدارًا كالخط والسطح والجسم؛ إذ هي أعظام ومقادير على خلاف الزمان والعدد؛ فإنه مما لا وضع لها. وقول من قال: «إن الزمان مقدار الحركة»، فالمراد منه كمية الحركة مطلقا لا المقدار الذي هو كم ذو وضع، أما المتصل فإنه أمر مشترك منه ما هو فصل الكم، ومنه ما هو عرض يلحق الأعظام من حيث هي أعظام، ومنه ما هو عرض يلحقها من حيث هي طبيعة.
Continuous quantity; rather it is better to say that it is an expression of that in which can be found that which counts something else, whether that be in potentiality as in magnitudes, or in actuality as in numbers. And it (i.e., quantity) is mentioned after substance for reasons that necessitate it: among them is participation in properties; and participation from what was mentioned before, and among them is that quantity is more general in existence than quality and more established in existence than relation. As for it being more general, because number is from quantity; and its existence is not restricted to things associated with motion and matter, excluding separate things that do not accept quality, nor anything outside their essence. And as for it being more established, because relation is not settled in the essence of its subject as quantity is settled, and also continuous quantity exists in all natural bodies without variation, while qualities vary in them. And quantity, when it shares with quality in substances, then it attaches to the first of them, which is body, while qualities attach to the lower and intermediate specific substances after corporeality. And it can be said from other aspects, but occupying oneself with that is occupying oneself with what does not suffice; and from the investigations concerning it is whether it is substance or accident, for if it is an expression of corporeality that accompanies matter, then it is fitting that it be a constitutive form. And the constitutive form of substances is substance, but it is not for the logician to address such, or to explain it in a definitive manner; its explanation was in another discipline, but he must say that body cannot be except that it is finite, but the definition of body insofar as it is body is other than the definition of finite body. Therefore body may be understood without finiteness unless one proves with a proof that affirms it; so finiteness is not included in the essence of body, so the surface is not part of its definition. Nevertheless, even though every body is finite, dimensions are not necessarily actual in body, for the sphere insofar as it is a sphere is a body and distinct dimensions are not assumed in it, rather body is body because it is in its nature such that three dimensions intersecting absolutely at a single limit at right angles can be assumed in it; and this is the corporeal form. So body is that in which the three dimensions intersecting at right angles can be assumed, and the corporeal form whose form is substantial, in which one body does not exceed another, so it is the form of substance or substance, unlike what estimates the three dimensions with a finite or infinite measure. For that is the accident that belongs to quantity, and one body may exist such that it may differ in respect of quantity and not differ in respect of form, like the candle that is shaped in a certain shape then changed to a different shape. So the proportion of dimensions in this shape differs from the proportion in that one. Even though corporeality and the candle remain as they were, so the corporeality that belongs to quantity follows the corporeality that is the form, because body necessitates determination, and the corporeal form when stripped of its quantity or when quantity is stripped from it taken in the mind is called a mathematical body. And it may be defined as the dimension divisible in three directions intersecting at a single limit at right angles; and it is abstract length, width, and depth in reality. Then quantity is divided into continuous and discrete, and also into having position and not having position. Having position is that its parts, with continuity, have a connection such that they can be pointed to by sensory indication, and it is called bulk and magnitude, like line, surface, and body; for they are bulks and magnitudes unlike time and number; for they have no position. And the statement of whoever said: 'Time is the magnitude of motion,' its meaning is the quantity of motion absolutely, not the magnitude which is quantity having position. As for the continuous, it is a common matter, part of which is the differential of quantity, part of which is an accident attaching to bulks insofar as they are bulks, and part of which is an accident attaching to them insofar as they are nature.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage defines quantity (al-kammiyya) and explains its placement after substance in the list of categories. The author begins by refining the definition of continuous quantity: it is not merely what is extended, but that in which something else can be counted, either potentially (as in magnitudes) or actually (as in numbers). Then he gives reasons why quantity is discussed after substance: (1) it shares some properties with substance, (2) it is more general in existence than quality (since numbers exist even in immaterial substances), and (3) it is more established in existence than relation (since relation is less fixed in its subject). The author argues that continuous quantity exists in all natural bodies without variation, while qualities vary. He then discusses the relationship between quantity and substance: quantity attaches first to body (the first substance in the physical world), while qualities attach to lower specific substances. He warns that it is not the logician's job to determine whether quantity is substance or accident—that belongs to another science (metaphysics). However, the logician must accept that body is necessarily finite, but finiteness is not part of the definition of body as such. Body is defined by the possibility of assuming three perpendicular dimensions. This 'corporeal form' (al-sūra al-jismiyya) is substantial and does not vary between bodies; what varies is the specific measure (accidental quantity). The example of a candle shaped differently illustrates that corporeality remains while quantity changes. When quantity is abstracted in the mind, we get the 'mathematical body' (jism taʿlīmī), defined as dimension divisible in three perpendicular directions. The passage then divides quantity into continuous/discrete (muttasil/munfaṣil) and into having position (dhū waḍʿ) and not having position. Having position means parts can be sensorily pointed to; this applies to line, surface, body (magnitudes), but not to time and number. Finally, the author clarifies that when time is called the 'magnitude of motion', it means quantity of motion, not the magnitude with position. The continuous is then said to be a common notion that can be the differential of quantity, an accident of bulks qua bulks, or an accident of them qua nature.

Key Terms
كمية
كيفية
مضاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is quantity said to be 'more established' (aṣaḥḥ) than relation (al-muḍāf)?
A) Because quantity is always actual, while relation is potential.
B) Because relation is not fixed in the subject as quantity is.
C) Because quantity is more general in existence.
D) Because relation belongs only to discrete entities.

Key Takeaway
Quantity is the first accident after substance because it is more general than quality and more stable than relation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the author's criteria, explain why number is more general in existence than quality. Provide an example from Islamic theology (e.g., angels have number but not qualities like heat).
?
Compare the conceptual relationship of quantity and relation: give a concrete example from fiqh where a quantity (e.g., amount of zakat) is more stable than a relation (e.g., kinship tie).

The Ten Categories

Body, Corporeal Form, and Mathematical Body
Author distinguishes body, corporeal form, and mathematical body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between corporeal form (al-sūra al-jismiyya) and mathematical body (jism taʿlīmī) as presented in the passage.

Core Idea
Body is defined by potential dimensions; actual measure is accidental.

The author makes a crucial distinction between the substantial form that makes a body a body (corporeal form) and the accidental quantity that gives it specific dimensions. He also introduces the concept of mathematical body—an abstraction in the mind. Understanding these distinctions is essential for avoiding confusion in physics and metaphysics.

Guided Path
1
Find the definition of body (jism)
The author says: 'الجسم هو الذي يمكن أن تفرض فيه الأبعاد الثلاثة المتقاطعة على زوايا قائمة'. Body is defined by the possibility of assuming three perpendicular dimensions.
Note that it is potentiality, not actuality.
2
Identify the corporeal form (al-sūra al-jismiyya)
The author describes it as the substantial form that does not vary among bodies; it is the form of substance itself. Locate: 'الصورة الجسمية التي صورتها الجوهرية التي لا يزيد فيها جسم على جسم'.
This form is common to all bodies, unlike specific quantitative shapes.
3
Distinguish accidental quantity from corporeal form
The example of a candle reshaped shows that the corporeal form remains while the dimensions (accidental quantity) change. Find this example and understand the point.
The candle retains its corporeality even when reshaped.
4
Understand the mathematical body (jism taʿlīmī)
When the mind strips away actual quantity from the corporeal form, it abstracts the mathematical body: dimension divisible in three perpendicular directions. Locate the definition: 'البعد القابل للتجزئة في ثلاث جهات متقاطعة...'.
This is an intellectual construct, not a physical entity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

يعقل الجسم دون التناهي ما لم يبرهن ببرهان يقرره؛ فالتناهي لا يكون داخلا في ماهية الجسم فالسطحي ليس جزءًا لحده. ومع ذلك فإنه وإن كان كل جسم متناهيًا فالأبعاد ليس بواجب حصولها في الجسم بالفعل فإن الكرة من حيث هي كرة جسم وليس تفرض فيها أبعاد متميزة بل الجسم إنما هو جسم؛ لأنه في طباعه بحيث يمكن أن تفرض فيه ثلاثة أبعاد متقاطعة على الإطلاق على حد واحد تقاطعًا على قوائم؛ وهذه هي الصورة الجسمية. فالجسم إذن هو الذي يمكن أن تفرض فيه الأبعاد الثلاثة المتقاطعة على زوايا قائمة والصورة الجسمية التي صورتها الجوهرية التي لا يزيد فيها جسم على جسم فهي صورة الجوهر أو جوهر بخلاف ما يقدر الأبعاد الثلاثة بقدر محدود أو غير محدود. فإن ذلك هو العرض الذي من باب الكمية والجسم الواحد قد يوجد بحيث يعرض له أن يختلف بحسب الكمية ولا يختلف بحسب الصورة مثل الشمعة التي يشكلها بشكل معين ثم يغيره إلى شكل بخلاف الأول. فنسبة الأبعاد في هذا الشكل مخالفة للنسبية في ذلك. وإن كانت الجسمية والشمعة باقية كما كانت فتكون الجسمية التي من باب الكمية تلزم الجسمية التي هي الصورة لما أن الجسم يلزمه التحدد وتكون الصورة الجسمية إذا جردت بكميتها أو جردت منها الكمية مأخوذة في الذهن يسمى المجرد جسما تعليمياً. وقد يعرف بأنه البعد القابل للتجزئة في ثلاث جهات متقاطعة على حد واحد تقاطعًا قائميًّا؛ وهو مجرد الطول والعرض والعمق في الحقيقة. ثم الكم ينقسم إلى متصل ومنفصل، وكذلك إلى ذي وضع وغير ذي وضع؛ وذو الوضع هو أن يكون لأجزائه مع الاتصال بقرر يمكن أن يشار إليه إشارة حسية، ويسمى عظما ومقدارًا كالخط والسطح والجسم؛ إذ هي أعظام ومقادير على خلاف الزمان والعدد؛ فإنه مما لا وضع لها. وقول من قال: «إن الزمان مقدار الحركة»، فالمراد منه كمية الحركة مطلقا لا المقدار الذي هو كم ذو وضع، أما المتصل فإنه أمر مشترك منه ما هو فصل الكم، ومنه ما هو عرض يلحق الأعظام من حيث هي أعظام، ومنه ما هو عرض يلحقها من حيث هي طبيعة.
Continuous quantity; rather it is better to say that it is an expression of that in which can be found that which counts something else, whether that be in potentiality as in magnitudes, or in actuality as in numbers. And it (i.e., quantity) is mentioned after substance for reasons that necessitate it: among them is participation in properties; and participation from what was mentioned before, and among them is that quantity is more general in existence than quality and more established in existence than relation. As for it being more general, because number is from quantity; and its existence is not restricted to things associated with motion and matter, excluding separate things that do not accept quality, nor anything outside their essence. And as for it being more established, because relation is not settled in the essence of its subject as quantity is settled, and also continuous quantity exists in all natural bodies without variation, while qualities vary in them. And quantity, when it shares with quality in substances, then it attaches to the first of them, which is body, while qualities attach to the lower and intermediate specific substances after corporeality. And it can be said from other aspects, but occupying oneself with that is occupying oneself with what does not suffice; and from the investigations concerning it is whether it is substance or accident, for if it is an expression of corporeality that accompanies matter, then it is fitting that it be a constitutive form. And the constitutive form of substances is substance, but it is not for the logician to address such, or to explain it in a definitive manner; its explanation was in another discipline, but he must say that body cannot be except that it is finite, but the definition of body insofar as it is body is other than the definition of finite body. Therefore body may be understood without finiteness unless one proves with a proof that affirms it; so finiteness is not included in the essence of body, so the surface is not part of its definition. Nevertheless, even though every body is finite, dimensions are not necessarily actual in body, for the sphere insofar as it is a sphere is a body and distinct dimensions are not assumed in it, rather body is body because it is in its nature such that three dimensions intersecting absolutely at a single limit at right angles can be assumed in it; and this is the corporeal form. So body is that in which the three dimensions intersecting at right angles can be assumed, and the corporeal form whose form is substantial, in which one body does not exceed another, so it is the form of substance or substance, unlike what estimates the three dimensions with a finite or infinite measure. For that is the accident that belongs to quantity, and one body may exist such that it may differ in respect of quantity and not differ in respect of form, like the candle that is shaped in a certain shape then changed to a different shape. So the proportion of dimensions in this shape differs from the proportion in that one. Even though corporeality and the candle remain as they were, so the corporeality that belongs to quantity follows the corporeality that is the form, because body necessitates determination, and the corporeal form when stripped of its quantity or when quantity is stripped from it taken in the mind is called a mathematical body. And it may be defined as the dimension divisible in three directions intersecting at a single limit at right angles; and it is abstract length, width, and depth in reality. Then quantity is divided into continuous and discrete, and also into having position and not having position. Having position is that its parts, with continuity, have a connection such that they can be pointed to by sensory indication, and it is called bulk and magnitude, like line, surface, and body; for they are bulks and magnitudes unlike time and number; for they have no position. And the statement of whoever said: 'Time is the magnitude of motion,' its meaning is the quantity of motion absolutely, not the magnitude which is quantity having position. As for the continuous, it is a common matter, part of which is the differential of quantity, part of which is an accident attaching to bulks insofar as they are bulks, and part of which is an accident attaching to them insofar as they are nature.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage defines quantity (al-kammiyya) and explains its placement after substance in the list of categories. The author begins by refining the definition of continuous quantity: it is not merely what is extended, but that in which something else can be counted, either potentially (as in magnitudes) or actually (as in numbers). Then he gives reasons why quantity is discussed after substance: (1) it shares some properties with substance, (2) it is more general in existence than quality (since numbers exist even in immaterial substances), and (3) it is more established in existence than relation (since relation is less fixed in its subject). The author argues that continuous quantity exists in all natural bodies without variation, while qualities vary. He then discusses the relationship between quantity and substance: quantity attaches first to body (the first substance in the physical world), while qualities attach to lower specific substances. He warns that it is not the logician's job to determine whether quantity is substance or accident—that belongs to another science (metaphysics). However, the logician must accept that body is necessarily finite, but finiteness is not part of the definition of body as such. Body is defined by the possibility of assuming three perpendicular dimensions. This 'corporeal form' (al-sūra al-jismiyya) is substantial and does not vary between bodies; what varies is the specific measure (accidental quantity). The example of a candle shaped differently illustrates that corporeality remains while quantity changes. When quantity is abstracted in the mind, we get the 'mathematical body' (jism taʿlīmī), defined as dimension divisible in three perpendicular directions. The passage then divides quantity into continuous/discrete (muttasil/munfaṣil) and into having position (dhū waḍʿ) and not having position. Having position means parts can be sensorily pointed to; this applies to line, surface, body (magnitudes), but not to time and number. Finally, the author clarifies that when time is called the 'magnitude of motion', it means quantity of motion, not the magnitude with position. The continuous is then said to be a common notion that can be the differential of quantity, an accident of bulks qua bulks, or an accident of them qua nature.

Division of Quantity: Continuous/Discrete and Position
Author divides quantity into continuous/discrete and with/without position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given quantity as continuous or discrete, and as having or not having position based on the author's criteria.

Core Idea
Continuous quantity with position is magnitude; without position it is time. Discrete quantity is number.

The passage concludes with a systematic division of quantity. Understanding these two cross-cutting distinctions helps you categorize any quantitative concept and apply it correctly in Islamic sciences like fiqh (time and number) and astronomy (magnitudes).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two divisions of quantity
The author divides quantity into continuous/discrete and into having/not having position. Find these in the Arabic: 'ثم الكم ينقسم إلى متصل ومنفصل، وكذلك إلى ذي وضع وغير ذي وضع'.
The divisions are independent but intersect.
2
Define 'having position' (dhū waḍʿ)
The author says having position means parts have a connection that can be sensorily pointed to. Example: line, surface, body (magnitudes). Locate: 'ذو الوضع هو أن يكون لأجزائه مع الاتصال بقرر يمكن أن يشار إليه إشارة حسية'.
Sensory indication means parts occupy distinct locations in space.
3
Identify examples of continuous quantities with and without position
Continuous with position: line, surface, body (called 'bulk' or 'magnitude'). Continuous without position: time. Find where time is mentioned as not having position.
Time is continuous because its moments flow, but parts do not coexist in space.
4
Identify discrete quantity and its position status
Discrete quantity is number (al-ʿadad). Number does not have position because its parts (units) are not spatially connected. Confirm from the passage that number has no position.
The author says 'فإنه مما لا وضع لها' referring to number and time.
5
Clarify the meaning of 'time as magnitude of motion'
The author corrects a common saying: time is the magnitude (quantity) of motion, not the magnitude with position. Understand the distinction between quantity of motion and positional magnitude.
The phrase 'مقدار الحركة' means the measure of motion, not a spatial magnitude.

الكمية المتصلة؛ بل الأولى أن يقال إنها عبارة عما يمكن أن يوجد فيه ما يعد غيره سواء كان ذلك بالقوة كما في المقادير، أو بالفعل كما في الأعداد. وإنها أي الكمية تذكر عقب الجوهر لمعان مستدعيه: منها المشاركة في الخواص؛ والمشاركة مما تقدم ذكره من قبل، ومنها أن الكمية أعم وجودًا من الكيفية وأصح وجودًا من المضاف. أما أنه أعم فلأن العدد من الكمية؛ وليس مقصورًا في وجوده على الأمور المقارنة للحركة والمادة دون المفارقة التي لا تقبل كيفية، ولا شيئًا خارجًا عن جوهرها. وأما أنها أصح فلأن المضاف غير متقرر في ذات موضوعه تقرر الكمية، وأيضًا فإن الكمية المتصلة توجد في جميع الأجسام الطبيعية من غير اختلاف، والكيفيات مختلفة فيها. والكمية إذا شاركت الكيفية في الجواهر فإنها تلزم الأول منها وهو الجسم، والكيفيات تلزم الجواهر النوعية السافلة والمتوسطة بعد الجسمية. ويمكن أن يقال من الوجوه ما عداها لكن الاشتغال بذلك اشتغال بما لا يغنيه؛ ولا يتم من المباحث فيها هو أنها جوهر أو عرض فإنها إذا كانت عبارة عن الجسمية التي تقارن المادة فبالحري أن تكون صورة مقومة. والصورة المقومة للجواهر جوهر لكن لا على المنطقي أن يتعرض لمثل هذا، أو يبينه على سبيل التحقيق؛ وقد كان بيانه في صناعة أخرى لكن يلزمه أن يقول إن الجسم لا يكون إلا وأن يكون متناهيا لكن حد الجسم من حيث هو جسم غير حد الجسم المتناهي. ولذلك قد يعقل الجسم دون التناهي ما لم يبرهن ببرهان يقرره؛ فالتناهي لا يكون داخلا في ماهية الجسم فالسطحي ليس جزءًا لحده. ومع ذلك فإنه وإن كان كل جسم متناهيًا فالأبعاد ليس بواجب حصولها في الجسم بالفعل فإن الكرة من حيث هي كرة جسم وليس تفرض فيها أبعاد متميزة بل الجسم إنما هو جسم؛ لأنه في طباعه بحيث يمكن أن تفرض فيه ثلاثة أبعاد متقاطعة على الإطلاق على حد واحد تقاطعًا على قوائم؛ وهذه هي الصورة الجسمية. فالجسم إذن هو الذي يمكن أن تفرض فيه الأبعاد الثلاثة المتقاطعة على زوايا قائمة والصورة الجسمية التي صورتها الجوهرية التي لا يزيد فيها جسم على جسم فهي صورة الجوهر أو جوهر بخلاف ما يقدر الأبعاد الثلاثة بقدر محدود أو غير محدود. فإن ذلك هو العرض الذي من باب الكمية والجسم الواحد قد يوجد بحيث يعرض له أن يختلف بحسب الكمية ولا يختلف بحسب الصورة مثل الشمعة التي يشكلها بشكل معين ثم يغيره إلى شكل بخلاف الأول. فنسبة الأبعاد في هذا الشكل مخالفة للنسبية في ذلك. وإن كانت الجسمية والشمعة باقية كما كانت فتكون الجسمية التي من باب الكمية تلزم الجسمية التي هي الصورة لما أن الجسم يلزمه التحدد وتكون الصورة الجسمية إذا جردت بكميتها أو جردت منها الكمية مأخوذة في الذهن يسمى المجرد جسما تعليمياً. وقد يعرف بأنه البعد القابل للتجزئة في ثلاث جهات متقاطعة على حد واحد تقاطعًا قائميًّا؛ وهو مجرد الطول والعرض والعمق في الحقيقة. ثم الكم ينقسم إلى متصل ومنفصل، وكذلك إلى ذي وضع وغير ذي وضع؛ وذو الوضع هو أن يكون لأجزائه مع الاتصال بقرر يمكن أن يشار إليه إشارة حسية، ويسمى عظما ومقدارًا كالخط والسطح والجسم؛ إذ هي أعظام ومقادير على خلاف الزمان والعدد؛ فإنه مما لا وضع لها. وقول من قال: «إن الزمان مقدار الحركة»، فالمراد منه كمية الحركة مطلقا لا المقدار الذي هو كم ذو وضع، أما المتصل فإنه أمر مشترك منه ما هو فصل الكم، ومنه ما هو عرض يلحق الأعظام من حيث هي أعظام، ومنه ما هو عرض يلحقها من حيث هي طبيعة.
Continuous quantity; rather it is better to say that it is an expression of that in which can be found that which counts something else, whether that be in potentiality as in magnitudes, or in actuality as in numbers. And it (i.e., quantity) is mentioned after substance for reasons that necessitate it: among them is participation in properties; and participation from what was mentioned before, and among them is that quantity is more general in existence than quality and more established in existence than relation. As for it being more general, because number is from quantity; and its existence is not restricted to things associated with motion and matter, excluding separate things that do not accept quality, nor anything outside their essence. And as for it being more established, because relation is not settled in the essence of its subject as quantity is settled, and also continuous quantity exists in all natural bodies without variation, while qualities vary in them. And quantity, when it shares with quality in substances, then it attaches to the first of them, which is body, while qualities attach to the lower and intermediate specific substances after corporeality. And it can be said from other aspects, but occupying oneself with that is occupying oneself with what does not suffice; and from the investigations concerning it is whether it is substance or accident, for if it is an expression of corporeality that accompanies matter, then it is fitting that it be a constitutive form. And the constitutive form of substances is substance, but it is not for the logician to address such, or to explain it in a definitive manner; its explanation was in another discipline, but he must say that body cannot be except that it is finite, but the definition of body insofar as it is body is other than the definition of finite body. Therefore body may be understood without finiteness unless one proves with a proof that affirms it; so finiteness is not included in the essence of body, so the surface is not part of its definition. Nevertheless, even though every body is finite, dimensions are not necessarily actual in body, for the sphere insofar as it is a sphere is a body and distinct dimensions are not assumed in it, rather body is body because it is in its nature such that three dimensions intersecting absolutely at a single limit at right angles can be assumed in it; and this is the corporeal form. So body is that in which the three dimensions intersecting at right angles can be assumed, and the corporeal form whose form is substantial, in which one body does not exceed another, so it is the form of substance or substance, unlike what estimates the three dimensions with a finite or infinite measure. For that is the accident that belongs to quantity, and one body may exist such that it may differ in respect of quantity and not differ in respect of form, like the candle that is shaped in a certain shape then changed to a different shape. So the proportion of dimensions in this shape differs from the proportion in that one. Even though corporeality and the candle remain as they were, so the corporeality that belongs to quantity follows the corporeality that is the form, because body necessitates determination, and the corporeal form when stripped of its quantity or when quantity is stripped from it taken in the mind is called a mathematical body. And it may be defined as the dimension divisible in three directions intersecting at a single limit at right angles; and it is abstract length, width, and depth in reality. Then quantity is divided into continuous and discrete, and also into having position and not having position. Having position is that its parts, with continuity, have a connection such that they can be pointed to by sensory indication, and it is called bulk and magnitude, like line, surface, and body; for they are bulks and magnitudes unlike time and number; for they have no position. And the statement of whoever said: 'Time is the magnitude of motion,' its meaning is the quantity of motion absolutely, not the magnitude which is quantity having position. As for the continuous, it is a common matter, part of which is the differential of quantity, part of which is an accident attaching to bulks insofar as they are bulks, and part of which is an accident attaching to them insofar as they are nature.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage defines quantity (al-kammiyya) and explains its placement after substance in the list of categories. The author begins by refining the definition of continuous quantity: it is not merely what is extended, but that in which something else can be counted, either potentially (as in magnitudes) or actually (as in numbers). Then he gives reasons why quantity is discussed after substance: (1) it shares some properties with substance, (2) it is more general in existence than quality (since numbers exist even in immaterial substances), and (3) it is more established in existence than relation (since relation is less fixed in its subject). The author argues that continuous quantity exists in all natural bodies without variation, while qualities vary. He then discusses the relationship between quantity and substance: quantity attaches first to body (the first substance in the physical world), while qualities attach to lower specific substances. He warns that it is not the logician's job to determine whether quantity is substance or accident—that belongs to another science (metaphysics). However, the logician must accept that body is necessarily finite, but finiteness is not part of the definition of body as such. Body is defined by the possibility of assuming three perpendicular dimensions. This 'corporeal form' (al-sūra al-jismiyya) is substantial and does not vary between bodies; what varies is the specific measure (accidental quantity). The example of a candle shaped differently illustrates that corporeality remains while quantity changes. When quantity is abstracted in the mind, we get the 'mathematical body' (jism taʿlīmī), defined as dimension divisible in three perpendicular directions. The passage then divides quantity into continuous/discrete (muttasil/munfaṣil) and into having position (dhū waḍʿ) and not having position. Having position means parts can be sensorily pointed to; this applies to line, surface, body (magnitudes), but not to time and number. Finally, the author clarifies that when time is called the 'magnitude of motion', it means quantity of motion, not the magnitude with position. The continuous is then said to be a common notion that can be the differential of quantity, an accident of bulks qua bulks, or an accident of them qua nature.

لا وضع له

Defining Body through Its Essential Form
Author defines body by three intersecting dimensions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of body in terms of three intersecting dimensions and identify the essential bodily form.

Core Idea
The bodily form is not quantity but the very essence of being a body.

In Islamic logic, body (jism) is defined not by its size or shape but by its essential form consisting of three dimensions intersecting at right angles. This passage introduces that definition and distinguishes the essential form from accidental quantity. Understanding this is foundational for further discussions on substance and accident in Kalam and falsafa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key components of the definition
Look for the phrase 'three dimensions intersecting at right angles' (ثلاثة أبعاد متقاطعة على قوائم). These dimensions constitute the bodily form (الصورة الجسمية).
2
Understand the definiendum and definiens
The author defines body as that in which three dimensions can be assumed. This is a real definition (hadd) indicating the essential property that makes a substance a body.
Compare with other definitions: some define body as a substance that receives dimensions.
3
Distinguish essential form from quantity
Note that the bodily form is separate from the quantity (الكمية) which is accidental. The essential form does not change when quantity varies, as shown in the candle example.
This distinction is crucial for later debates on atomism and continuity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثلاثة أبعاد متقاطعة على الإطلاق على حد واحد تقاطعًا على قوائم؛ وهذه هي الصورة الجسمية. فالجسم إذن هو الذي يمكن أن تفرض فيه الأبعاد الثلاثة المتقاطعة على زوايا قائمة والصورة الجسمية التي صورتها الجوهرية التي لا يزيد فيها جسم على جسم فهي صورة الجوهر أو جوهر بخلاف ما يقدر الأبعاد الثلاثة بقدر محدود أو غير محدود. فإن ذلك هو العرض الذي من باب الكمية والجسم الواحد قد يوجد بحيث يعرض له أن يختلف بحسب الكمية ولا يختلف بحسب الصورة مثل الشمعة التي يشكلها بشكل معين ثم يغيره إلى شكل بخلاف الأول. فنسبة الأبعاد في هذا الشكل مخالفة للنسبية في ذلك. وإن كانت الجسمية والشمعة باقية كما كانت فتكون الجسمية التي من باب الكمية تلزم الجسمية التي هي الصورة لما أن الجسم يلزمه التحدد وتكون الصورة الجسمية إذا جردت بكميتها أو جردت منها الكمية مأخوذة في الذهن يسمى المجرد جسماً تعليمياً. وقد يعرف بأنه البعد القابل للتجزئة في ثلاث / جهات متقاطعة على حد واحد تقاطعًا قائميًّا؛ وهو مجرد الطول والعرض والعمق في الحقيقة.
Three dimensions intersecting absolutely at one point at right angles; and this is the bodily form. So body is that in which three dimensions intersecting at right angles can be assumed, and the bodily form is its essential form by which one body does not exceed another; it is the form of substance or substance, unlike estimating the three dimensions by a limited or unlimited measure. For that is the accident from the category of quantity. And a single body may exist such that it varies according to quantity but does not vary according to form, like a candle that is shaped in a particular shape and then changed to a shape different from the first. So the proportion of dimensions in this shape is different from the proportion in that, even though the body-ness and the candle remain as they were. Thus the body-ness from the category of quantity accompanies the body-ness that is the form, because the body necessarily has delimitation. And the bodily form, if stripped of its quantity or quantity is stripped from it, taken in the mind, is called in abstraction a 'teaching body' (jism ta'līmī). It may be defined as the dimension divisible in three directions intersecting at one point at right angles; and it is simply length, width, and depth in reality.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is a definition of body (jism) in the context of the category of quantity. The author begins by stating that three dimensions intersecting at right angles constitute the bodily form (al-sūra al-jismiyya). Body is therefore defined as that in which these three dimensions can be assumed. The author then distinguishes between the essential form (al-sūra al-jawhariyya) of a body, which makes it a body regardless of size, and the accidental quantity (al-kamm) that can vary. Using the example of a candle that changes shape, the author shows that the essential bodily form remains even when quantity changes. The author explains that bodily form from the category of quantity necessarily accompanies the essential form because any body requires delimitation. When quantity is abstracted in the mind, it becomes the 'teaching body' (jism ta'līmī), which is the pure mathematical concept of three-dimensional extension. Finally, a definition is given: body is the dimension divisible in three mutually perpendicular directions, i.e., length, width, and depth.

جسم
البعد

C) Its substantiality
D) Its divisibility

Key Takeaway
Body is defined by its essential form—three dimensions intersecting at right angles—and this form is distinct from accidental quantity.

Distinguishing Essential Form from Accidental Quantity
Author distinguishes essential bodily form from accidental quantity and introduces jism ta'līmī.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between the essential form of a body and its accidental quantity using the candle example, and define the abstract 'teaching body' (jism ta'līmī).

Core Idea
The candle's shape changes, but its bodily form remains—quantity is accidental.

The author uses a simple candle to illustrate a profound logical distinction: the essential form (al-ṣūra al-jawhariyya) that makes a body a body does not change when its quantity (al-kammiyya) varies. This leads to the concept of jism ta'līmī, the purely mathematical abstraction of extension. Understanding this distinction is critical for later discussions on the nature of substance and accident.

Guided Path
1
Examine the candle example
The author describes a candle that can be reshaped from one form to another. Note that the material (the candle) and its body-ness remain, while the proportions change.
2
Identify the two types of 'body-ness'
The passage distinguishes between the essential bodily form (الصورة الجسمية) and the quantity body-ness (الجسمية من باب الكمية). The essential form is invariant; the quantity varies.
The quantity body-ness necessarily accompanies the essential form because every body must have some delimitation.
3
Understand the abstraction of jism ta'līmī
When quantity is stripped from the bodily form in the mind, we get the 'teaching body'—a pure three-dimensional extension that is the subject of geometry.
This abstraction is used in mathematics but is not the actual physical body.

The author explains that bodily form from the category of quantity necessarily accompanies the essential form because any body requires delimitation. When quantity is abstracted in the mind, it becomes the 'teaching body' (jism ta'līmī), which is the pure mathematical concept of three-dimensional extension. Finally, a definition is given: body is the dimension divisible in three mutually perpendicular directions, i.e., length, width, and depth.

Key Terms

D) The accidental quantity of a body

Classifying Quantity: Continuous, Discrete, and Position
Author classifies quantity into continuous/discrete and with/without position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish continuous from discrete quantity and identify which quantities have position, including the three types of continuous.

Core Idea
Quantity is divided by continuity, discreteness, and position; true continuity requires a shared boundary.

In this passage, the author presents the fundamental division of quantity into continuous and discrete, and adds a second division based on whether the quantity has position (i.e., its parts can be pointed to sensorily). Understanding these divisions is essential for defining and reasoning about physical entities like body, line, and time in Islamic philosophical and scientific texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary division
Note that quantity (al-kamm) is first divided into continuous (muttasil) and discrete (munfasil). The author then adds a second division: with position (dhū waḍ‘) and without position (ghayr dhī waḍ‘).
Position here means that the parts have a sensory arrangement.
2
Understand 'with position'
A quantity with position has parts that can be pointed to by sensory indication. Examples given: line, surface, body. These are called magnitudes (miqdār). Time and number lack position.
The author clarifies that when time is called 'measure of motion', it means quantity of motion absolutely, not a magnitude with position.
3
Distinguish the three types of continuous
The author identifies three senses of 'continuous': (1) the genuine continuous where a common boundary can be assumed between parts; (2) continuous by actual shared edge (e.g., two lines meeting at a point); (3) continuous by motion (e.g., parts that separate during movement). The first is the true continuous that divides quantity.
Only the first type is essential to quantity; the others are accidental.
4
Apply to body
Body has a common boundary (surface) among its parts, similar to how surface shares a line and line shares a point. However, sensible body is divisible only as a quantity (magnitude), not as body absolutely.
This shows how the classification of continuous quantity applies to physical objects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الكم ينقسم إلى متصل ومنفصل، وكذلك إلى ذي وضع وغير ذي وضع ؛ وذو الوضع هو أن يكون لأجزائه مع الاتصال بقرر يمكن أن يشار إليه إشارة حسية، ويسمى عظما ومقدارًا كالخط والسطح والجسم؛ إذ هي أعظام ومقادير على خلاف الزمان والعدد؛ فإنه مما لا وضع لها. وقول من قال: «إن الزمان مقدار الحركة»، فالمراد منه كمية الحركة مطلقا لا المقدار الذي هو كم ذو وضع، أما المتصل فإنه أمر مشترك منه ما هو فصل الكم، ومنه ما هو عرض يلحق الأعظام من حيث هي أعظام، ومنه ما هو عرض يلحقها من حيث هي طبيعة. أما الأول فهو الذي يمكن أن يفرض بين أجزائه حد مشترك هو نهاية البعض منها، ومن خاصته أنه يقال على المقدار الواحد في نفسه من غير أن يتصل إلى الغير، ولا يشترط فيه أن يكون هناك قطع بالفعل بل الشرط فيه أن يكون هناك إمكان هذا التوهم فهذا هو المتصل الذي ينقسم إليه الكم وإلى المنفصل. وأما الثاني فهو الذي يقال على ما يوجد فيه طرف ونهاية واحدة بالفعل هي بعينها طرف لما قيل إنه متصل به حتى لو كانتا نهايتين لكان إمكان الاتصال مماسة مثل الخط الذي يتصل بخط على زاوية يحده نقطة واحدة بالفعل هي طرف لهما جميعًا، ومثل الجسم الذي له جزءان مختلفان كما أن أحدهما موضوع للسواد والآخر للبياض مثلا، والنهايتان هما نهاية واحدة في الحقيقة وإن كانتا نهايتين في العرض؛ فهذا من الاتصالات ما يكون للكميات وهي كميات لا يلحقها إلا لواحق الكميات مثل هيئة الوضع لخطي الزاوية مثلاً. وأما الثالث فهو أن يكون المتصل به فلأن ما للمتصل في حركته التي يزول بها طرف الذي يليه عن موضعه الذي يلاقي بنهايته فيه؛ وهذا الاتصال لا يمتنع أن يكون فيه مماسة إذا وجدت فلازمه عند الحركة. والاتصال الحقيقي بحسب هذا الموضع هو الأول وإن كان إنما يقال اسمه من الاتصال على سبيل الإضافة إذا كانت توهم له أجزاء فيما بينها الاتصال الإضافي. وكثيرًا ما ينقل اسم لشيء عن اسم شيء آخر فيصير بحسب صناعة ما لحق بذلك الاسم، فالجسم هو من هذا الباب لما أنه بحيث يمكن أن يفرض بين أجزائه حد مشترك وهو السطح، وكما أن أجزاءه تشترك بالسطح فكذلك السطح بالخط والخط بالنقطة؛ وكذلك الزمان بالآن. ثم الجسم المحسوس لا يتجزأ إلا من حيث هو ذو كم وهو المقدار لا من حيث إنه جسم على الإطلاق.
Then quantity is divided into continuous and discrete, and also into that which has position and that which does not have position. That which has position is that its parts, along with continuity, can be arranged such that it can be pointed to by sensory indication, and it is called 'magnitude' and 'quantity', like line, surface, and body; for they are magnitudes and quantities, unlike time and number; for they have no position. And the statement of one who said: 'Time is the measure of motion' — the intended meaning is the quantity of motion absolutely, not the magnitude that is a quantity with position. As for the continuous, it is a common notion: among it is what is the differentia of quantity, and among it is what is an accident that attaches to magnitudes insofar as they are magnitudes, and among it is what is an accident that attaches to them insofar as they are nature. As for the first: it is that in which a common boundary can be assumed between its parts, which is the end of some of them. Its property is that it is said of a single quantity in itself without connecting to another, and it is not necessary that there be actual division; rather the condition is that there be possibility of this supposition. This is the continuous that divides quantity into it and the discrete. As for the second: it is that which is said of that in which there actually exists an edge and a single end that is itself the edge of what is said to be continuous with it, such that even if there were two ends, the possibility of continuity would be contact, like a line connected to a line at an angle bounded by a single point actually that is an edge for both of them, and like a body that has two different parts, as if one is the subject of blackness and the other of whiteness, and the two ends are in reality one end even if they are two ends in appearance. This is among the connectives that belong to quantities, and they are quantities that only the concomitants of quantity attach to them, like the configuration of position for two lines at an angle, for instance. As for the third: it is that the connected is such because of its motion by which its adjacent edge departs from its place that it meets with its end; this connection does not preclude that there be contact when it occurs, and its necessary concomitant during motion. The true connection according to this position is the first, even though its name is derived from connection by way of addition when parts are imagined to have an additive connection among themselves. And often the name of a thing is transferred from the name of another thing, and it becomes according to some discipline attached to that name. Body is of this category, because it is such that a common boundary can be assumed between its parts, which is the surface, and just as its parts share the surface, so too the surface shares the line, and the line shares the point; and likewise time shares the 'now'. Then the sensible body is not divisible except insofar as it has quantity, i.e., magnitude, not insofar as it is body absolutely.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage classifies quantity (al-kamm) and explains the types of continuous quantity. The author first divides quantity into continuous (muttasil) and discrete (munfasil), and also into that which has position (dhū waḍ‘) and that which does not (ghayr dhī waḍ‘). Position is defined as having parts arranged in a sensory-identifiable manner. Examples of quantity with position are line, surface, and body (magnitudes), while time and number lack position. The author clarifies that when time is called 'measure of motion', it refers to the quantity of motion absolutely, not a magnitude with position. The concept of 'continuous' is then divided into three types: (1) the genuine continuous, where a common boundary can be assumed between parts (e.g., a line segment where the endpoint is shared); this is the essential differentia of quantity. (2) The continuous in the sense of being connected at an actual single edge, as in two lines meeting at a point or a body with two differently colored parts sharing a boundary; the ends are actually one. (3) The continuous due to motion, where parts are connected but may have contact when movement occurs. The author states that the true continuous is the first type, though the name may be transferred to others. Finally, the author applies this to body: body has a common boundary (surface) among its parts, analogous to surface sharing points with lines. However, sensible body is divisible only as a quantity (magnitude), not as body itself.

الوضع

The Definition of Continuous Quantity (al-Muttasil)
Definition of the first type of continuous quantity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition for a quantity to be continuous and distinguish it from discrete quantity based on the possibility of a common boundary.

Core Idea
Continuous quantity is defined by the possibility of a common boundary between its parts, not by actual division.

In this lesson, we examine the definition of the first type of continuous quantity (al-muttasil) as presented in classical Islamic logic. The author clarifies its essential characteristic: the possibility of imagining a common boundary between its parts. This definition distinguishes continuous from discrete quantity. Understanding this foundational concept is crucial for later discussions of quantity in metaphysics and natural philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined in the Arabic passage. It is 'المتصل' (al-muttasil), meaning the continuous.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus is 'quantity' (الكم). The differentia is 'the possibility of supposing a common boundary between its parts' (إمكان فرض حد مشترك بين أجزائه).
3
Examine the condition
Note that the author explicitly rejects the requirement of actual division; only possibility (إمكان) is necessary.
The author says: 'ولا يشترط فيه أن يكون هناك قطع بالفعل بل الشرط فيه أن يكون هناك إمكان هذا التوهم'.
4
Apply the definition
Test with an example: a line is continuous because you can imagine a point as a boundary between its segments. A group of chairs is discrete because no common boundary exists.
Use Islamic examples: time from Fajr to Maghrib is continuous; number of rak'at in a prayer is discrete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الأول فهو الذي يمكن أن يفرض بين أجزائه حد مشترك هو نهاية البعض منها، ومن خاصته أنه يقال على المقدار الواحد في نفسه من غير أن يتصل إلى الغير، ولا يشترط فيه أن يكون هناك قطع بالفعل بل الشرط فيه أن يكون هناك إمكان هذا التوهم فهذا هو المتصل الذي ينقسم إليه الكم وإلى المنفصل.
As for the first, it is that between whose parts it is possible to suppose a common boundary that is the end of some of them, and of its property is that it is predicated of a single quantity in itself without it connecting to another, and it is not conditioned that there be actual division, rather the condition is that there be the possibility of this supposition. So this is the continuous to which quantity and the discrete are divided.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage defines the first type of continuous quantity (al-muttasil) within the category of quantity. The author begins by stating that this type is characterized by the possibility of imagining a common boundary (hadd mushtarak) between its parts, which is the endpoint of some of them. For example, a line has points that serve as boundaries for segments. Its specific property (khassa) is that it applies to a single magnitude in itself without being attached to another; that is, it is not defined by actual connection to something else. The author clarifies that actual division (qaṭʿ bi-l-fiʿl) is not required; rather, the mere possibility of such supposition (tawahhum) suffices. This definition distinguishes continuous quantity from discrete quantity (al-munfasil), and the passage concludes that quantity as a category is divided into the continuous and the discrete.

Key Terms
المتصل
الحد المشترك

Key Takeaway
Continuous quantity is defined by the possibility, not actuality, of a common boundary between its parts.

Second and Third Types of Continuous Connection
Author defines the second and third types of continuous connection with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the second and third types of continuous connection and classify examples from Ilm al-Hay'a.

Core Idea
Not all continuity is created equal: some share one endpoint, others are born from motion.

We have seen that continuous connection (ittisāl) can occur when two things share a common boundary. But what if they share exactly one endpoint? And what if motion is involved? This passage from the logic text defines two more types, refining our understanding beyond the ordinary definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second type's defining feature
Find where the author says 'second' (الثاني) and look for what makes this type unique: a single actual endpoint (طرف واحد بالفعل) shared by both connected things.
Even if there were two endpoints, the connection would be contact (مماسة), not this type.
2
Illustrate with the line example
The author gives the example of a line connecting to another line at an angle. They share one point that is the endpoint for both. Visualize this as the vertex of an angle.
This is a standard geometrical example in the tradition.
3
Identify the third type's defining feature
Find the mention of 'third' (الثالث). This type involves motion: the connected thing moves and displaces the endpoint of the adjacent thing from its position.
The author says contact (مماسة) may occur as a concomitant of motion.
4
Compare contact vs. shared endpoint
Contrast the second type (shared actual endpoint) with contact (mumāssa), which would require two separate endpoints. Understand that the second type is a stronger form of continuity.
The third type allows contact to appear because motion can create a temporary adjacency.
5
Classify examples from Islamic astronomy
Apply the definitions: When two celestial spheres share a boundary surface that is one in reality, it is the second type. When one sphere rotates and pushes against another, causing displacement of the adjacent sphere's endpoint, it is the third type.
These examples are from Ilm al-Hay'a (astronomy).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثاني فهو الذي يقال على ما يوجد فيه طرف ونهاية واحدة بالفعل هي بعينها طرف لما قيل إنه متصل به حتى لو كانتا نهايتين لكان إمكان الاتصال مماسة مثل الخط الذي يتصل بخط على زاوية يحده نقطة واحدة بالفعل هي طرف لهما جميعًا، ومثل الجسم الذي له جزءان مختلفان كما أن أحدهما موضوع للسواد والآخر للبياض مثلا، والنهايتان هما نهاية واحدة في الحقيقة وإن كانتا نهايتين في العرض؛ فهذا من الاتصالات ما يكون للكميات وهي كميات لا يلحقها إلا لواحق الكميات مثل هيئة الوضع لخطي الزاوية مثلاً. وأما الثالث فهو أن يكون المتصل به فلأن ما للمتصل في حركته التي يزول بها طرف الذي يليه عن موضعه الذي يلاقي بنهايته فيه؛ وهذا الاتصال لا يمتنع أن يكون فيه مماسة إذا وجدت فلازمه عند الحركة.
As for the second, it is that which is predicated of that in which there exists an endpoint and a single actual boundary that is itself the endpoint for what is said to be connected to it, even if there were two endpoints, the possibility of connection would be contact, like a line that connects to a line at an angle, a single point actually bounds it and is an endpoint for both of them, and like the body that has two different parts, as one is a subject for blackness and the other for whiteness, for example, and the two endpoints are one endpoint in reality even if they are two endpoints accidentally; so this is from among the connections that belong to quantities, and they are quantities that are only affected by the accidents of quantities, such as the disposition of position of two lines of an angle for example. As for the third, it is that the connected to is such that what is for the connected in its motion removes the endpoint of that which is adjacent from its position that it meets with its endpoint; and this connection does not preclude that there be contact in it, if it exists, then it is a concomitant of motion.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage defines two additional types of continuous connection. The second type (al-thānī) applies when two connected things share a single actual endpoint (ṭaraf wāḥid bi-l-fiʿl). For example, two lines meeting at an angle are bounded by a single point that is the endpoint for both. Similarly, a body with two differently colored parts shares one boundary surface. In reality, it is one endpoint, though accidentally it may appear as two. This connection is proper to quantities and is affected only by accidents of quantity, such as configuration (hayʾa al-waḍʿ). The third type (al-thālith) involves motion: when a moving connected thing displaces the endpoint of an adjacent thing from its position. In this connection, contact (mumāssa) may occur as a concomitant of motion. These distinctions refine the concept of continuity beyond the primary definition.

Key Terms
المماسة
هيئة الوضع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It involves a single actual endpoint shared by two things.
B) It requires motion.
C) It has no common boundary.
D) It applies only to lines.

Key Takeaway
The second type of continuous connection shares one actual endpoint; the third type arises from motion and may involve contact.

Two Types of Continuity
Author distinguishes real from additional continuity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between real continuity (al-ittiṣāl al-ḥaqīqī) and additional continuity (al-ittiṣāl al-iḍāfī) and identify which type is used in a given context.

Core Idea
Real continuity requires a common boundary; additional continuity is a transferred name.

The author clarifies that the word 'continuous' can be used in two ways: one real, the other relational. This distinction is crucial when analyzing definitions of quantities like body, line, and time, as it prevents confusion between literal and metaphorical uses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of continuity
The author says the real continuous (al-ittiṣāl al-ḥaqīqī) is the first type, where parts share a common boundary. The name is also used for additional continuity (al-ittiṣāl al-iḍāfī) when parts are supposed to have a connection without a real boundary.
Additional continuity is a transferred name, not the original meaning.
2
Understand the condition for real continuity
Real continuity requires a common boundary (ḥadd mushtarak) between parts, such as a surface connecting two parts of a body.
3
Recognize additional continuity
Additional continuity occurs when the name 'continuous' is used for things where parts are imagined to be connected, like time and the now, even though no physical boundary exists.
This is an example of naql al-ism (transfer of name).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاتصال الحقيقي بحسب هذا الموضع هو الأول وإن كان إنما يقال اسمه من الاتصال على سبيل الإضافة إذا كانت توهم له أجزاء فيما بينها الاتصال الإضافي. وكثيرًا ما ينقل اسم لشيء عن اسم شيء آخر فيصير بحسب صناعة ما لحق بذلك الاسم، فالجسم هو من هذا الباب لما أنه بحيث يمكن أن يفرض بين أجزائه حد مشترك وهو السطح، وكما أن أجزاءه تشترك بالسطح فكذلك السطح بالخط والخط بالنقطة؛ وكذلك الزمان بالآن.
And real connection according to this position is the first, even though its name is said from connection by way of addition, if parts are supposed for it, then between them there is additional connection. And often a name is transferred from one thing to another and becomes, according to a certain art, attached to that name. So body is of this chapter, because it is such that it is possible to suppose a common boundary between its parts, which is the surface, and just as its parts share in the surface, so too the surface shares in the line, and the line in the point; and likewise time in the now.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage first concludes that the real continuous (al-ittiṣāl al-ḥaqīqī) according to this context is the first type defined earlier (the one with a possible common boundary), even though the name 'continuous' is sometimes used in an additional sense (bi-sabīl al-iḍāfa) when parts are imagined with a connection between them. Then the author discusses the transfer of names (naql al-ism) from one thing to another, and how a name becomes attached to a thing in a particular art (ṣināʿa). Body (jism) is an example: it is called continuous because between its parts a common boundary (surface) can be supposed. Similarly, surface shares in line, line in point, and time in the now (ān). This illustrates how the concept of continuity extends analogically through different kinds of quantities.

Key Terms
الاتصال الحقيقي
الاتصال الإضافي
نقل الاسم

A) The name is transferred from another thing.
B) A common boundary exists between parts.
C) The thing has three dimensions.

Key Takeaway
Real continuity requires a common boundary; otherwise, the term is used in a transferred sense.

Name Transfer and the Chain of Continuity
Author illustrates transfer of names with body and other quantities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the name 'continuous' transfers from one thing to another and apply this logic to examples from Islamic philosophy.

Core Idea
Names transfer by analogy through shared boundaries across different types of quantities.

The author illustrates naql al-ism with the example of body, surface, line, point, and time. The name 'continuous' travels through these by a chain of common boundaries. This logical pattern is important for understanding how terms are extended in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the chain of transfer
The author says body is continuous because its parts are connected by a common boundary (surface). Similarly, surface shares in line, line in point, time in the now. This is a sequence of analogical transfers.
2
Understand the mechanism of transfer
Each step transfers the name 'continuous' because the latter thing shares a boundary with the former. For example, surface is continuous with line because they share a line as boundary.
This mechanism is based on the idea of a common boundary (ḥadd mushtarak).
3
Recognize the broader application of naql al-ism
The author states that often a name is transferred from one thing to another and becomes attached in a particular art (ṣināʿa). This is a general logical principle used in defining terms across disciplines.
In uṣūl al-fiqh, naql al-ism is used for sharʿī terms; here it is a general concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاتصال الحقيقي بحسب هذا الموضع هو الأول وإن كان إنما يقال اسمه من الاتصال على سبيل الإضافة إذا كانت توهم له أجزاء فيما بينها الاتصال الإضافي. وكثيرًا ما ينقل اسم لشيء عن اسم شيء آخر فيصير بحسب صناعة ما لحق بذلك الاسم، فالجسم هو من هذا الباب لما أنه بحيث يمكن أن يفرض بين أجزائه حد مشترك وهو السطح، وكما أن أجزاءه تشترك بالسطح فكذلك السطح بالخط والخط بالنقطة؛ وكذلك الزمان بالآن.
And real connection according to this position is the first, even though its name is said from connection by way of addition, if parts are supposed for it, then between them there is additional connection. And often a name is transferred from one thing to another and becomes, according to a certain art, attached to that name. So body is of this chapter, because it is such that it is possible to suppose a common boundary between its parts, which is the surface, and just as its parts share in the surface, so too the surface shares in the line, and the line in the point; and likewise time in the now.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage first concludes that the real continuous (al-ittiṣāl al-ḥaqīqī) according to this context is the first type defined earlier (the one with a possible common boundary), even though the name 'continuous' is sometimes used in an additional sense (bi-sabīl al-iḍāfa) when parts are imagined with a connection between them. Then the author discusses the transfer of names (naql al-ism) from one thing to another, and how a name becomes attached to a thing in a particular art (ṣināʿa). Body (jism) is an example: it is called continuous because between its parts a common boundary (surface) can be supposed. Similarly, surface shares in line, line in point, and time in the now (ān). This illustrates how the concept of continuity extends analogically through different kinds of quantities.

Key Takeaway
The name 'continuous' transfers through quantities by a chain of common boundaries: body via surface, surface via line, line via point, and time via the now.

Cause of Divisibility: Matter vs Quantity
Author presents and answers an objection about the cause of divisibility in body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the positions in a dialectical exchange about the cause of divisibility and explain the author's response regarding the logician's scope.

Core Idea
Quantity, not matter, is the direct cause of divisibility in body.

This passage presents a compact dialectical exchange: the author asserts that body is divisible only due to quantity, an objector counters that matter is the real cause because it receives division, and the author responds by distinguishing the logical perspective from the physical. Understanding this exchange trains you to recognize how logicians delimit their subject matter from that of other sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's initial claim
Find the statement about when body is divisible. The author says 'al-jism al-maḥsūs lā yatajazzaʾ illā min ḥaythu huwa dhū kamm wa-huwa al-miqdār, lā min ḥaythu innahu jism ʿalā al-iṭlāq.' This means: the sensed body is divisible only insofar as it possesses quantity, not insofar as it is body absolutely.
The key is the distinction between 'body absolutely' and 'body as having quantity'.
2
Extract the objection
The objector says: division only occurs according to something that can receive division; that is matter (mādda). Therefore division is due to matter, not quantity. Note the structure: 'al-tajzīʾah lā takūn illā bi-ḥasab shayʾ min shaʾnihi an yaqbala al-tajzīʾah; wa-dhālika huwa al-mādda.'
The objector assumes the cause must be the receptive principle.
3
Analyze the author's response
The author concedes that matter participates, but insists that division 'occurs to magnitude insofar as it is magnitude.' Then he adds that the logician does not need to investigate matter's specific role. This is a delimitation: logic deals with quantity as such.
The author shifts the discussion from physics (matter) to logic (quantity).
4
Assess the dialectical outcome
The author does not deny matter's role but argues that the logician's focus is on the formal aspect (quantity). The objection is deflected by scoping – the logician is not required to resolve every metaphysical detail.
This is a common move in Islamic logic: distinguishing the proper subject matter of each science.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الجسم المحسوس لا يتجزأ إلا من حيث هو ذو كم وهو المقدار لا من حيث إنه جسم على الإطلاق. ولقائل أن يقول التجزئة لا تكون إلا بحسب شيء من شأنه / أن يقبل التجزئة؛ وذلك هو المادة فتكون التجزئة بسبب المادة لا بسبب الكمية، لكنا نقول إن التجزئة تعرض للمقدار بما هو مقدار وإن كان للمادة فيه مشاركة. وأما أن خُصّة المادة في ذلك ما هي فلا يجب على المنطقي أن يشتغل بذلك.
Then the sensed body is not divisible except inasmuch as it possesses quantity, i.e., magnitude, not inasmuch as it is body absolutely. And an objector may say: division only occurs according to something that is capable of receiving division; that is matter, so division is due to matter, not due to quantity. But we say: division occurs to magnitude insofar as it is magnitude, even if matter participates in it. As for what specifically matter does in that, it is not necessary for the logician to occupy himself with it.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange regarding the cause of divisibility in body. The author first states that a sensed body (al-jism al-maḥsūs) is divisible only insofar as it has quantity (kamm), i.e., magnitude (miqdār), not insofar as it is body absolutely. Then an objector (qāʾil) raises a challenge: division requires something that receives division, which is matter (mādda); therefore division is due to matter, not quantity. The author responds (lākin naqūl) that division occurs to magnitude precisely as magnitude, though matter participates. However, the specific role of matter is not the logician's concern; the logician deals with quantity as such. This response delimits the scope of logical inquiry.

Key Terms
المادة
المقدار

Key Takeaway
The logician focuses on quantity as the formal cause of divisibility, leaving the material cause to physics.

Discrete Quantity: Logical Nature and Definition by Property
Author defines discrete quantity (number) and provides a specific definition of each number.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish discrete quantity from continuous quantity using the criterion of common boundary, and identify a definition by property (ta'rif bi-l-khassa) in logical texts.

Core Idea
Number is defined not by genus and differentia, but by a distinctive property — half the sum of its two sides.

In Islamic logic, quantities are divided into continuous and discrete. This passage focuses on discrete quantity (number) and provides a unique definition by property. You will learn how to parse this definition and understand its logical status, which is foundational for categories used in many Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the criterion of discrete quantity
Look for the phrase 'لا يمكن أن يفرض بين أجزائه حد واحد مشترك' — the defining characteristic is that parts cannot share a common boundary.
This criterion distinguishes discrete from continuous quantity.
2
Apply the criterion to the example of nine
The author divides nine into three and four and shows that no common unit can exist as a boundary. Understand the numerical reasoning: if a shared unit existed, it would have to be part of the number's own units (leading to six total) or external (leading to eight), both impossible.
3
Extract the definition of number
Find the statement 'عبارة عما يساوي نصف مجموع خاشيتها'. Note that 'khāshiyatayhi' means its two sides — typically the numbers immediately before and after it. For example, seven is half of 6+8, also half of 5+9, 4+10, etc.
This is a property that holds for every number.
4
Recognize definition by property (ta'rif bi-l-khassa)
The author explicitly says 'وهذا هو التعريف بالخاصة'. Compare this with a definition by genus and differentia (hadd haqiqi). Here, the property (khassa) is used as a defining characteristic, not the essence.
Definition by property is a common logical tool in Islamic sciences.
5
Consider the subtle issue of number as accident
The author notes a dispute over whether number is an accident but asserts that it suffices to know that the unit is an accident and the sum of accidents is an accident.
This shows awareness of metaphysical implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المنفصل فهو الذي لا يمكن أن يفرض بين أجزائه حد واحد مشترك يتلاقى عنده ويتحد به وهو العدد ليس إلا كالتسعة مثلا لا يكون لأجزائها حد مشترك، فإنها إذا جزئت إلى ثلاثة وأربعة لم يوجد بينهما طرف مشترك. فإنه لا طرف للأعداد إلا الوحدة ولا توجد الوحدة المشتركة بينهما. ولو وجدت وكانت تلك الوحدة من وحداتها كانت الوحدات ستة. ولو كانت من غيرها كانت الوحدات ثمانية كذلك ؛ ولا مجال لهذا ولا لذلك. ثم العدد يعرف بأنه عبارة عما يساوي نصف مجموع خاشيتها كالسبعة فإنها نصف المجموع الحاصل من الستة والثمانية، والحاصل أيضًا من الخمسة والتسعة أو من الأربعة والعشرة ؛ وهذا هو التعريف بالخاصة. فإن من خواص العدد أن كل فرد منه بهذه الصفة. وقد يشكل في العدد أيضًا أنه عرض أو ليس بعرض لكن ما يكون من اللوازم معرفته هو أن الوحدة في ذوات الوحدة عرض خارج عن ماهيتها، وأن مجموع الأعراض عرض كذلك.
As for the discrete, it is that between whose parts it is not possible to suppose a common boundary where they meet and unite; it is number only, like nine, e.g., its parts have no common boundary. For if it is divided into three and four, no common endpoint exists between them. For there is no endpoint for numbers except the unit, and the common unit does not exist between them. If it existed and that unit was from its units, the units would be six; and if it was from others, the units would be eight likewise; there is no scope for this or that. Then number is defined as that which equals half of the sum of its two sides (khāshiyatayhi); like seven, it is half of the sum obtained from six and eight, and also obtained from five and nine, or from four and ten. And this is the definition by property (bi-l-khāṣṣa). For among the properties of number is that every single one has this description. And it may be problematic regarding number whether it is an accident or not an accident, but that which is necessary to know is that the unit in the essences of the unit is an accident outside its essence, and that the sum of accidents is an accident as well.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage defines discrete quantity (al-munfaṣil) as number (ʿadad), which cannot have a common boundary between its parts. For example, dividing nine into three and four yields no shared unit endpoint. The author explains that if there were a shared unit, it would have to be either part of the number's units (leading to six units) or external (leading to eight), both impossible. Then a definition of each number is given: every number is half the sum of its two 'sides' (khāshiyatayhi), meaning the numbers immediately before and after it in the natural order, or also other pairs that sum to twice the number. For seven, half of 6+8=14 gives 7, also 5+9=14, 4+10=14, etc. This is a definition by property (taʿrīf bi-l-khāṣṣa), not by genus and differentia. Finally, a subtle issue is raised: whether number is an accident. The author says it suffices to know that the unit is an accident, and the sum of accidents is an accident.

Key Terms
المنفصل (al-munfaṣil)
العدد (al-ʿadad)
التعريف بالخاصة (al-taʿrīf bi-l-khāṣṣa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the definition of number given in this passage?
A) A collection of units.
B) That which equals half the sum of its two sides.
C) A continuous quantity.
D) A divisible magnitude.

Key Takeaway
Discrete quantity (number) is defined by the absence of a common boundary between its parts, and each number can be defined by the property of being half the sum of its two adjacent numbers, which is a definition by property, not by genus and differentia.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
