The Two Types of Quantity: Stationary and Successive
Author divides quantity into stationary (magnitude) and successive (time).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish stationary quantity (magnitude) from successive quantity (time) as defined by the logicians.

Core Idea
Quantity is either constantly present (magnitude) or renewing itself (time).

The author returns to the category of quantity and draws a fundamental division: it is either stationary in essence or successive. This distinction clarifies the logician's scope—magnitude is fully his concern, while time is delegated to natural science. Understanding this division helps you see how the logician categorizes the world into stable and changing quantities.

Guided Path
1
Locate the main division
Find the two-part distinction in the Arabic: 'إما أن يكون قار الذات فيما هو كم له، وإما أن لا يكون بل في التجدد'. Note that 'قار الذات' (stationary in essence) and 'التجدد' (renewal) are the two branches.
2
Define stationary quantity
Stationary quantity is also called magnitude (عظم) and measure (قدر). It is extension (امتداد) that does not change in its existence. The author will later subdivide it into line, surface, and body.
Think of a fixed length or volume that remains as it is.
3
Define successive quantity
Successive quantity is time (زمان). It is described as a form of motion (هيئة حركة) that is a state of body, i.e., it inheres in moving bodies. Time is not stable but renews moment by moment.
Time is not a substance but an accident of motion.
4
Understand the logician's limitation
The author says 'ليس على المنطقي أن يبينه' — the detailed explanation of time belongs to natural science, not logic. The logician only needs to classify it as a quantity; the physicist explains its reality.
This shows the boundaries of logic: it deals with concepts, not physical proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والآن نرجع إلى الكم فنقول إنه إما أن يكون قار الذات فيما هو كم له، وإما أن لا يكون بل في التجدد، وما يكون على سبيل التجدد فذلك هيئة حركة هي حالة جسم لا محالة؛ وذلك هو الزمان وبيانه في العلم الطبيعي فلا على المنطقي أن يبينه . وأما القار فهو الذي يسمى عظما وقدرًا كما مر ، وإنه إما أن يكون امتداده بُعدًا واحدًا لا يقبل التجزئة إلا في جهة واحدة وهو الخط،
And now we return to quantity and say: it is either stationary in essence with respect to what it has of quantity, or not, but rather in renewal. And what is in the manner of renewal is a form of motion that is necessarily a state of body; and that is time, and its elucidation is in natural science, so it is not for the logician to explain it. As for the stationary, it is called magnitude (ʿiẓam) and quantity (qadr) as previously, and it is such that its extension is a single dimension that does not admit division except in one direction, which is the line.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage divides quantity (al-kamm) into two kinds: stationary (qār al-dhāt) and successive (fī al-tajaddud). Stationary quantity is that which is stable in its essence, like magnitude (ʿiẓam) or measure (qadr). Successive quantity is something that is in a state of renewal (tajaddud), which is a form of motion (hayʾa ḥaraka) inhering in body; this is time (zamān). The author notes that the explanation of time belongs to natural science (ʿilm ṭabīʿī), not to logic. Then, within stationary quantity, the author begins to list the types: extension (imtidād) of a single dimension divisible only in one direction, i.e., the line. The text ends here, but it is clear that the author will continue with surface and body.

Key Terms
قار الذات
التجدد
الزمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Continuous quantity of magnitude.

Key Takeaway
The logician divides quantity into stationary (magnitude) and successive (time), and leaves the physical explanation of time to natural science.

Divisibility and the Definition of Discrete Quantity
Author addresses objection about divisibility and defines discrete quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify discrete quantity and its defining characteristic (no common boundary between parts) while distinguishing divisibility due to quantity from divisibility due to matter.

Core Idea
Divisibility belongs to magnitude as such, not merely matter; discrete quantity is defined by the absence of a common boundary.

The passage begins with an objection: divisibility is due to matter, not quantity. The author responds that divisibility belongs to magnitude as such, and the logician need not investigate matter's role. Then he defines discrete quantity (al-munfasil) as quantity whose parts have no common boundary, and identifies it with number. This lesson clarifies the nature of discrete quantity and the priority of quantity over matter in division.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Locate in the Arabic the objection that divisibility is because of matter, not quantity. The objector claims divisibility requires something to receive division (matter), so divisibility belongs to matter.
This is a potential fallacy of confusing the material condition with the formal cause.
2
Extract the author's response
The author counters that divisibility belongs to the magnitude insofar as it is a magnitude, even if matter participates. He restricts the logician's concern to the formal aspect.
This shows the logician's focus on the quantitative aspect, not the material substrate.
3
Define discrete quantity
The author defines discrete quantity (al-munfasil) as that in which one cannot posit a common boundary between its parts. The example of nine divided into three and four illustrates no common boundary.
This contrasts with continuous quantity (al-muttasil) which has a common boundary (e.g., a line).
4
Identify the term, genus, and differentia
The term being defined is 'discrete quantity' (al-munfasil). The genus is 'quantity' (al-kammiyya). The differentia is 'having no common boundary between its parts'. This is a description (rasm), not an essential definition.
In logic, a proper definition (hadd) requires genus and differentia; here only a description is given.
5
Apply to an Islamic science example
In fiqh, the number of rak'at in prayer is a discrete quantity; each rak'a is separate with no common boundary. The obligation of prayer depends on the number being discrete, not continuous.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولقائل أن يقول التجزئة لا تكون إلا بحسب شيء من شأنه أن يقبل التجزئة؛ وذلك هو المادة فتكون التجزئة بسبب المادة لا بسبب الكمية، لكنا نقول إن التجزئة تعرض للمقدار بما هو مقدار وإن كان للمادة فيه مشاركة. وأما أن خُصّة المادة في ذلك ما هي فلا يجب على المنطقي أن يشتغل بذلك. وأما المنفصل فهو الذي لا يمكن أن يفرض بين أجزائه حد واحد مشترك يتلاقى عنده ويتحد به وهو العدد ليس إلا كالتسعة مثلا لا يكون لأجزائها حد مشترك، فإنها إذا جزئت إلى ثلاثة وأربعة لم يوجد بينهما طرف مشترك. فإنه لا طرف للأعداد إلا الوحدة ولا توجد الوحدة المشتركة بينهما. ولو وجدت وكانت تلك الوحدة من وحداتها كانت الوحدات ستة. ولو كانت من غيرها كانت الوحدات ثمانية كذلك؛ ولا مجال لهذا ولا لذلك. ثم العدد يعرف بأنه عبارة عما يساوي نصف مجموع خاشيتيه كالسبعة فإنها نصف المجموع الحاصل من الستة والثمانية، والحاصل أيضًا من الخمسة والتسعة أو من الأربعة والعشرة؛ وهذا هو التعريف بالخاصة. فإن من خواص العدد أن كل فرد منه بهذه الصفة. وقد يشكل في العدد أيضًا أنه عرض أو ليس بعرض لكن ما يكون من اللوازم معرفته هو أن الوحدة في ذوات الوحدة عرض خارج عن ماهيتها، وأن مجموع الأعراض عرض كذلك.
And someone may say: Divisibility only occurs according to something that is capable of receiving division; that is matter. Thus divisibility is due to matter, not due to quantity. But we say: Divisibility occurs to the magnitude insofar as it is a magnitude, even if matter participates in it. As for the particularity of matter in that, it is not necessary for the logician to occupy himself with it. As for the discrete: it is that in which it is not possible to posit between its parts one common boundary at which they meet and unite; it is number, like nine, for example, whose parts have no common boundary, for if it is divided into three and four, no common extremity is found between them. For there is no extremity for numbers except the unit, and the common unit between them does not exist. If it existed and that unit was from among its units, the units would be six; and if it was from other units, the units would be eight likewise; there is no room for this or that. Then number is defined as that which is equal to half the sum of its two sides, like seven, for it is half the sum resulting from six and eight, and also from five and nine, or from four and ten; this is definition by property. For it is among the properties of number that every individual of it is of this description. And there may be a problem concerning number also: whether it is an accident or not an accident. But what is necessary to know among its concomitants is that the unit in the essences of the unit is an accident external to its quiddity, and that the collection of accidents is also an accident.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection about the divisibility of quantity: someone claims that divisibility is due to matter, not quantity. The author refutes this, stating that divisibility belongs to magnitude as such, though matter may be involved. The logician need not investigate matter's role. Then the author defines discrete quantity (al-munfasil), which is number. Discrete quantity is characterized by having no common boundary between its parts; for example, nine divided into three and four has no common point. The only boundary for numbers is the unit, but no common unit exists between the two groups. If a common unit were possible, it would lead to absurdities. The author then gives the definition of number by property (bi al-khassa): number is that which equals half the sum of its two adjacent numbers (e.g., 7 is half of 6+8, 5+9, 4+10). This is not the essence but a distinguishing property. Finally, the author notes a problem about whether number is an accident, concluding that the unit is an accident external to the quiddity of 'unit', and the collection of accidents is also an accident — meaning number is an accident.

Key Terms
التجزئة
المنفصل
العدد
الخاصة
العرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the defining characteristic of discrete quantity (al-munfasil)?
A) It has parts that share a common boundary.
B) It is divisible by matter.
C) It cannot be divided.
D) Its parts have no common boundary.

Key Takeaway
Discrete quantity is defined by the absence of a common boundary between its parts, and divisibility is primarily a property of quantity, not matter.

Defining Number by Property and Its Accidental Nature
Author gives property definition of number and discusses its accidental status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the property-based definition of number as given in this passage (half the sum of adjacent numbers) and analyze the claim that number is an accidental entity.

Core Idea
Number is defined by a special property, not its essence, and it is considered an accident external to quiddity.

After defining discrete quantity as number, the author provides a definition of number by property (bi al-khassa): number equals half the sum of its two adjacent numbers. He then raises a problem about whether number is an accident, concluding that the unit (the building block of number) is an accident external to the quiddity of the unit, and since a collection of accidents is also an accident, number is an accident. This lesson dissects this property definition and the ontological status of number.

Guided Path
1
Locate the property definition
Find in the Arabic where the author says 'number is defined as that which is equal to half the sum of its two sides'. Note the example: 7 is half of 6+8, 5+9, 4+10.
The phrase 'two sides' (khashiyatayhi) likely means the two adjacent numbers.
2
Understand that this is a definition by property (khassa)
The author explicitly says 'this is the definition by property' (bi al-khassa). A property is a non-essential attribute that distinguishes a thing but does not capture its essence. So this definition does not tell us what number is essentially, but it gives a distinguishing feature.
In logic, a proper definition (hadd) requires genus and differentia; here only a property is given, making it a rasm.
3
Analyze the ontological status: number as accident
The author notes a problem about whether number is an accident. He concludes that the unit (the individual unit) is an accident external to the quiddity (essence) of the unit. Since number is a collection of such accidental units, the collection of accidents is also an accident. So number is an accident.
This relates to the category of quantity being an accident in Aristotelian ontology.
4
Apply to an Islamic science example
In Islamic inheritance law (fara'id), the shares of heirs are given as fractions (1/2, 1/3, etc.) which are numbers. The fact that these numbers are accidents means they are not part of the essence of the heirs but added attributes. This affects how jurists reason about the ontological basis of shares.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولقائل أن يقول التجزئة لا تكون إلا بحسب شيء من شأنه أن يقبل التجزئة؛ وذلك هو المادة فتكون التجزئة بسبب المادة لا بسبب الكمية، لكنا نقول إن التجزئة تعرض للمقدار بما هو مقدار وإن كان للمادة فيه مشاركة. وأما أن خُصّة المادة في ذلك ما هي فلا يجب على المنطقي أن يشتغل بذلك. وأما المنفصل فهو الذي لا يمكن أن يفرض بين أجزائه حد واحد مشترك يتلاقى عنده ويتحد به وهو العدد ليس إلا كالتسعة مثلا لا يكون لأجزائها حد مشترك، فإنها إذا جزئت إلى ثلاثة وأربعة لم يوجد بينهما طرف مشترك. فإنه لا طرف للأعداد إلا الوحدة ولا توجد الوحدة المشتركة بينهما. ولو وجدت وكانت تلك الوحدة من وحداتها كانت الوحدات ستة. ولو كانت من غيرها كانت الوحدات ثمانية كذلك؛ ولا مجال لهذا ولا لذلك. ثم العدد يعرف بأنه عبارة عما يساوي نصف مجموع خاشيتيه كالسبعة فإنها نصف المجموع الحاصل من الستة والثمانية، والحاصل أيضًا من الخمسة والتسعة أو من الأربعة والعشرة؛ وهذا هو التعريف بالخاصة. فإن من خواص العدد أن كل فرد منه بهذه الصفة. وقد يشكل في العدد أيضًا أنه عرض أو ليس بعرض لكن ما يكون من اللوازم معرفته هو أن الوحدة في ذوات الوحدة عرض خارج عن ماهيتها، وأن مجموع الأعراض عرض كذلك.
And someone may say: Divisibility only occurs according to something that is capable of receiving division; that is matter. Thus divisibility is due to matter, not due to quantity. But we say: Divisibility occurs to the magnitude insofar as it is a magnitude, even if matter participates in it. As for the particularity of matter in that, it is not necessary for the logician to occupy himself with it. As for the discrete: it is that in which it is not possible to posit between its parts one common boundary at which they meet and unite; it is number, like nine, for example, whose parts have no common boundary, for if it is divided into three and four, no common extremity is found between them. For there is no extremity for numbers except the unit, and the common unit between them does not exist. If it existed and that unit was from among its units, the units would be six; and if it was from other units, the units would be eight likewise; there is no room for this or that. Then number is defined as that which is equal to half the sum of its two sides, like seven, for it is half the sum resulting from six and eight, and also from five and nine, or from four and ten; this is definition by property. For it is among the properties of number that every individual of it is of this description. And there may be a problem concerning number also: whether it is an accident or not an accident. But what is necessary to know among its concomitants is that the unit in the essences of the unit is an accident external to its quiddity, and that the collection of accidents is also an accident.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection about the divisibility of quantity: someone claims that divisibility is due to matter, not quantity. The author refutes this, stating that divisibility belongs to magnitude as such, though matter may be involved. The logician need not investigate matter's role. Then the author defines discrete quantity (al-munfasil), which is number. Discrete quantity is characterized by having no common boundary between its parts; for example, nine divided into three and four has no common point. The only boundary for numbers is the unit, but no common unit exists between the two groups. If a common unit were possible, it would lead to absurdities. The author then gives the definition of number by property (bi al-khassa): number is that which equals half the sum of its two adjacent numbers (e.g., 7 is half of 6+8, 5+9, 4+10). This is not the essence but a distinguishing property. Finally, the author notes a problem about whether number is an accident, concluding that the unit is an accident external to the quiddity of 'unit', and the collection of accidents is also an accident — meaning number is an accident.

العدد
الخاصة

Key Takeaway

Classifying Stationary Quantity: Line, Surface, and Body
Definitions of the three stationary quantities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of stationary quantity and state their definitions in terms of dimensions.

Core Idea
Stationary quantity is extension in one, two, or three dimensions: line, surface, body.

The author now turns to the classification of continuous quantity. He distinguishes stationary quantity, which is magnitude, from temporal quantity. Stationary quantity itself is divided into three kinds based on the number of dimensions: line (one), surface (two), and body (three). Let's examine each definition closely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
Find in the Arabic the terms for line (khaṭṭ), surface (saṭḥ), and body (jism ta‘līmī).
2
Note the dimension count
For each, note how many directions of division it admits: one, two, or three.
3
Extract the definitions
The author gives definitions from geometry: line is length without width; surface is length and width only; body is length, width, and depth.
4
Recognize the special usage
The body here is 'jism ta‘līmī' (geometrical body), distinct from physical substance.
In physics, jism often means substance; here it is purely quantitative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والآن نرجع إلى الكم فنقول إنه إما أن يكون قار الذات فيما هو كم له، وإما أن لا يكون بل في التجدد، وما يكون على سبيل التجدد فذلك هيئة حركة هي حالة جسم لا محالة؛ وذلك هو الزمان وبيانه في العلم الطبيعي فلا على المنطقي أن يبينه. وأما القار فهو الذي يسمى عظما وقدرًا كما مر، وإنه إما أن يكون امتداده بُعدًا واحدًا لا يقبل التجزئة إلا في جهة واحدة وهو الخط، والخط يرسم في مبادئ الهندسة بأنه طول ولا عرض له، وإما أن يكون امتداده بُعدًا لا يقبل التجزئة إلا في جهتين متقاطعتين على حد واحد؛ وهذا مما يمكن فيه فرض بعدين متناظرين على قوائم وهو السطح؛ والسطح يرسم بأنه طول وعرض فقط. وإما أن يكون بُعدًا هو قابل التجزئة في ثلاث جهات متقاطعة على حد واحد كما تقدم ذكره وهو الجسم التعليمي. وهذا هو الجسم الذي من باب الكم فيسمى جسمًا من حيث إنه بهذه الصفة وقد يسمى عمقًا وسمكًا وتحتا. أما عمقًا فلكونه تحتا نازلا، وأما سمكًا فلكونه تحتا صاعدًا، وأما تحتا فلكونه جسما بين السطوح، وربما وقع اسم العمق على معنى آخر سنذكره.
And now we return to quantity and say: It is either stationary in essence with respect to what is quantity for it, or it is not but rather in becoming; that which is in the mode of becoming is a form of motion, which is a state of body necessarily; that is time, and its explanation is in natural science, so it is not upon the logician to explain it. As for the stationary, it is that which is called magnitude and dimension, as previously mentioned; and it is either an extension of one dimension that does not admit division except in one direction, and that is the line; and the line is defined in the principles of geometry as length without width; or its extension is a dimension that does not admit division except in two intersecting directions on one boundary; this allows the positing of two symmetrical dimensions at right angles, and that is the surface; and the surface is defined as length and width only; or it is a dimension that admits division in three intersecting directions on one boundary, as previously mentioned, and that is the corporeal body. This is the body that belongs to the category of quantity, so it is called 'body' insofar as it has this description, and it may be called depth, thickness, and 'taht'. As for depth, because it is 'taht' descending; as for thickness, because it is 'taht' ascending; as for 'taht', because it is a body between surfaces; and perhaps the name 'depth' occurs for another meaning we will mention later.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage classifies continuous quantity (al-kamm al-muttasil). First, it distinguishes between stationary (qār al-dhāt) and non-stationary (time). Time is a form of motion and belongs to natural science, not logic. The stationary kind is magnitude ('izam or qadr). Magnitude is then subdivided by dimensions: one-dimensional is the line (khaṭṭ), defined as length without width; two-dimensional is the surface (saṭḥ), defined as length and width only; three-dimensional is the corporeal body (jism ta‘līmī), which is divisible in three directions. This body of quantity is called jism, and also 'depth' ('umq), 'thickness' (simak), and 'taht' (which seems to mean a body between surfaces but has directional nuances: depth descending, thickness ascending). The author notes that 'depth' may have another meaning later.

Key Terms

الخط
السطح
الجسم التعليمي
العمق
السمك

Key Takeaway
Stationary quantity is divided into line (one dimension), surface (two dimensions), and body (three dimensions), each defined by its number of dimensions.

Temporal Quantity: Time and Its Exclusion from Logic
Classification of continuous quantity into stationary and temporal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish stationary quantity from temporal quantity and explain why time belongs to natural science.

Core Idea
Time is a non-stationary quantity, a form of motion, and thus outside the scope of logic.

The author begins his discussion of continuous quantity by making a fundamental division: quantity is either stationary (qār) or in a state of becoming (tajaddud). The latter is time, which is a form of motion. Because time belongs to natural philosophy, the logician need not elaborate on it. This lesson clarifies that classification and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Locate the division
Find where the author splits quantity into stationary and non-stationary.
2
Identify the non-stationary
He calls it time, and says it is a form of motion.
3
Note the disavowal
The author says it is not upon the logician to explain time; it belongs to natural science.
4
Understand the reasoning
Time is not a static magnitude but a measure of change, so it falls under physics.

Critique of Relational Definitions of Place as Quantity
Author critiques definitions of place as continuous quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical flaw in defining a category (like place) by mixing relational terms with quantitative terms.

Core Idea
A definition mixing categories (relational and quantitative) fails to isolate the true nature of the defined thing.

In the classical Islamic logical tradition, definitions must be precise and not mix categories. The author here critiques a common definition of 'place' (al-makān) that attempts to classify it as a continuous quantity. He shows that the definition includes relational terms (al-muḍāf) which are not quantitative, thereby undermining the claim. This lesson walks through his critique, teaching you how to spot the same error in definitions used in other Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions under critique
Find the two definitions of place presented: (1) inner surface of containing body touching outer surface of contained body; (2) extremity of surrounding body, container of contained.
2
Extract the terms used in these definitions
List the terms: surface, inner, container, touching, outer, extremity. Note that these are the terms the author analyzes.
3
Classify each term as quantity or relation
Determine which terms are quantitative (surface) and which are relational (all others: inner, container, touching, outer, extremity). The author points out that only 'surface' is a quantity; the rest are muḍāf.
4
Analyze the logical consequence
The author presents a dilemma: if place is quantity because of relational terms, then relationality would confer quantity—absurd. If not, then only the surface is truly quantity, so the definition fails to define place as a distinct quantity. This shows the definition is flawed.
5
Generalize the lesson
Understand that definitions must avoid mixing categories (here, quantitative and relational). This critical method applies to any definition in Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن قوما زعموا أن المكان نوع من المتصل الذي يسمى عظما زائدا على السطح وعرفوه بأنه السطح الباطن للجسم الحاوي المماس للسطح الظاهر من الجسم المحوي. وقد يعبر بهذه العبارة أيضًا وهي أنه نهاية الجسم المحيط وحاو للمحاط وسطح في ذاته لكنه في حيز المنع فإن الداخل في التعريف الأول هو السطح والباطن والحاوي والمماس والظاهر؛ وجميع هذا من المضاف سوى السطح فكمية إذن لكونه سطحًا. وعلى هذا في التعريف الثاني إلى النهاية. والحاوي من المضاف أيضًا. فلو كان المكان كما لكونه نهاية وحاويًا لكان الشيء من حيث إنه مصاف هو من الكم ولأنه إذا كان كما. فإما أن يكون لكونه نهاية أو حاويًا مدخل في كونه كما؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون للمكان من حيث هو كم الكم الذي يفيد كونه نهاية وحاويًا خصوصيته قبول أبعاد غير الذي يفيده السطح. وأما أن لا يكون، وحينئذ هذه الجملة إنما هي من الكم لكون البعض منها أو موضوعها وهو السطح من الكم فيكون الكم بالحقيقة هو السطح. وأما غير القار وهو الزمان فهو مقدار حركة الفلك، والحد المشترك بين أجزائه المفروضة هو الآن؛ وذلك طرف الزمان على ما عرف.
Then some people claimed that place is a type of continuous quantity called magnitude, additional to surface, and they defined it as the inner surface of the containing body that touches the outer surface of the contained body. And it is also expressed by this expression: it is the extremity of the surrounding body and a container of the contained, and a surface in itself, but it is in the realm of prevention. For that which enters into the first definition is surface, inner, container, touching, and outer; all of these are relational except surface, so it is quantity due to being a surface. Similarly, in the second definition, up to the extremity. And 'container' is also relational. If place were quantity due to being an extremity and a container, then the thing insofar as it is relational would be from quantity, and because if it is quantity, then either being an extremity or a container has an entry in its being quantity; then it would follow that place, insofar as it is quantity, possesses a particularity of accepting dimensions other than those that surface provides. Or it does not; then this totality is from quantity because some of it or its subject, which is surface, is from quantity, so the quantity in reality is the surface. As for the non-stationary, which is time, it is the measure of the motion of the sphere, and the common boundary between its hypothetical parts is the 'now'; that is the extremity of time, as is known.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage first critiques a definition of place (makān) as a type of continuous quantity. Some defined it as the inner surface of the containing body in contact with the outer surface of the contained body. Another expression defines it as the extremity of the surrounding body and container of the contained. The author points out that these definitions rely on relational terms (muḍāf) like 'inner', 'container', 'touching', etc. Only 'surface' is a quantity; the rest are relations. Therefore, if place is quantity because of being an extremity or container, then it would be quantity due to relation, which is problematic. Either the relational aspects give place its quantitative nature (leading to absurd consequences), or they do not, in which case the true quantity is the surface, not place as defined. After this critique, the author briefly describes time (zamān) as non-stationary quantity, the measure of the motion of the sphere, with 'now' as the common boundary between its parts, analogous to the extremity of number.

المضاف
الزمان
الآن

D) Because place is actually time.

Key Takeaway
Definitions must avoid mixing relational terms with quantitative terms to maintain categorical purity.

Defining Continuous Quantity: Line, Surface, and Body
Author defines line, surface, and body as continuous quantities with geometric properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define line, surface, and teaching body and state the number of dimensions for each.

Core Idea
Line has length only; surface has length and width; body has length, width, and depth.

This lesson unpacks the definitions of the three types of continuous quantity from the principles of geometry. These definitions are foundational in Islamic logic for classifying spatial entities. You will learn to distinguish each type by its dimensional properties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
From the passage, locate the three types: line (khatt), surface (sath), and teaching body (jism ta'limi).
They are listed sequentially.
2
Extract dimensions
For each, note the number of dimensions mentioned: line has only length; surface has length and width; body has length, width, and depth.
Depth is also called umq, samk, or taht.
3
Understand additional terms for body
The passage gives synonyms for the third dimension: depth, thickness, undermess. These describe orientations.
Depth (umq) is descending, thickness (samk) is ascending, undermess (taht) is the body between surfaces.
4
Distinguish teaching body from physical body
The teaching body is a mathematical abstraction; it belongs to the category of quantity. Physical bodies also have other qualities.
This distinction is important in Islamic natural philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخط يرسم في مبادئ الهندسة بأنه طول ولا عرض له، وإما أن يكون امتداده بُعدًا لا يقبل التجزئة إلا في جهتين متقاطعتين على حد واحد؛ وهذا مما يمكن فيه فرض بعدين متناظرين على قوائم وهو السطح ; والسطح يرسم بأنه طول وعرض فقط . وإما أن يكون بُعدًا هو قابل التجزئة في ثلاث جهات متقاطعة على حد واحد كما تقدم ذكره وهو الجسم التعليمي. وهذا هو الجسم الذي من باب الكم فيسمى جسمًا من حيث إنه بهذه الصفة وقد يسمى عمقًا وسمكًا .وتحتا. أما عمقًا فلكونه تحتا نازلا، وأما سمكًا فلكونه تحتا صاعدًا، وأما تحتا فلكونه جسما بين السطوح، وربما وقع اسم العمق على معنى آخر سنذكره.
The line is defined in the principles of geometry as length without width. Its extension is a dimension that does not admit division except in two perpendicular directions; this is a surface, and the surface is defined as length and width only. Or the extension is a dimension divisible in three perpendicular directions, as previously mentioned, and that is the teaching body. This is the body that belongs to the category of quantity, so it is called 'body' insofar as it has this description, and it may be called 'depth' and 'thickness' and 'underness'. As for 'depth', because it is descending undermess; as for 'thickness', because it is ascending undermess; as for 'underness', because it is a body between surfaces. Sometimes the name 'depth' is applied to another meaning we will mention.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage defines the three types of continuous quantity: line, surface, and body (al-jism al-ta'limi). The author follows the geometric tradition: a line has length only, a surface has length and width, and a body (geometric solid) has length, width, and depth. The body is further described with terms like 'depth' (al-umq), 'thickness' (al-samk), and 'underness' (al-taht). These terms indicate different orientations of the third dimension. The passage ends by noting that 'depth' may also be used for another meaning, to be discussed later. This is a standard classification of continuous quantity in Islamic logic, building on Aristotle's Categories.

السطح

Key Takeaway
Continuous quantity is classified into line (one dimension), surface (two dimensions), and body (three dimensions), forming the basis for geometric reasoning in logic.

Critique of Defining Place as a Continuous Quantity
Author examines and critiques the view that place (makan) is a type of continuous quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a definition improperly mixes relational terms (al-mudaf) with quantitative terms, and critique such definitions using the logical criteria presented in the passage.

Core Idea
Relational terms do not make something a quantity – only the surface is truly quantitative.

In this passage, the author scrutinizes a definition of place (makan) offered by some scholars who claim it is a type of continuous quantity. The definition includes terms like 'inner surface', 'containing body', and 'touching'. The author argues that most of these are relational (al-mudaf) and not quantitative, so the definition fails to establish place as a distinct quantity. You will learn to apply this kind of logical dissection to definitions in Islamic science.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition and its components
Locate the two versions of the definition in the Arabic: (1) the inner surface of the containing body touching the outer surface of the contained body; (2) the limit of the surrounding body and container of the contained. List all terms used in the definitions.
Note the terms: surface, inner, container, touching, outer, limit, containing.
2
Distinguish quantitative from relational terms
Which terms are inherently quantitative (i.e., extension)? Only 'surface' qualifies as a continuous quantity. Terms like 'inner', 'container', 'touching' are relational (al-mudaf) – they refer to a relation between two things, not an inherent measurement.
Relational terms do not belong to the category of quantity (kamm).
3
Analyze the author's logical alternative
If place is quantity because of the relational aspects, then relational things would be quantity – a false conclusion. If it is quantity only because of the surface, then place adds no new type of quantity; it is just the surface itself. Thus the definition fails to establish place as a distinct quantity.
Trace the dilemma: either the relational terms contribute to 'being quantity' (absurd) or they do not (then quantity is just surface).
4
Apply the lesson to a similar case in Islamic science
Consider definitions of 'void' or 'time' in Kalām. Do they mix relational concepts with quantitative ones? For example, some define void as 'empty space'. The relational aspect 'empty' does not add a new quantity; only extension is quantitative.
Practice applying the critique to another definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن قوما زعموا أن المكان نوع من المتصل الذي يسمى عظما زائدا على [٥] السطح وعرفوه بأنه السطح الباطن / للجسم الحاوي المماس للسطح الظاهر من الجسم المحوي. وقد يعبر بهذه العبارة أيضًا وهي أنه نهاية الجسم المحيط وحاو للمحاط وسطح في ذاته لكنه في حيز المنع فإن الداخل في التعريف الأول هو السطح والباطن والحاوي والمماس والظاهر؛ وجميع هذا من المضاف سوى السطح فكمية إذن لكونه سطحًا. وعلى هذا في التعريف الثاني إلى النهاية. والحاوي من المضاف أيضًا. فلو كان المكان كما لكونه نهاية وحاويًا لكان الشيء من حيث إنه مصاف هو من الكم ولأنه إذا كان كما. فإما أن يكون لكونه نهاية أو حاويًا مدخل في كونه كما; وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون للمكان من حيث هو كم الكم الذي يفيد كونه ،نهاية ، وحاويًا خصوصيته قبول أبعاد غير الذي يفيده السطح. وأما أن لا يكون، وحينئذ هذه الجملة إنما هي من الكم لكون البعض منها أو موضوعها وهو السطح من الكم فيكون الكم بالحقيقة هو السطح.
Then some people claimed that place is a type of continuous quantity called 'bone' in addition to the surface, and they defined it as: the inner surface of the containing body that touches the outer surface of the contained body. And it is also expressed by this statement: it is the limit of the surrounding body and the container of the contained, and it is a surface in itself, but it is in the domain of prevention. For what enters into the first definition is: the surface, the inner, the container, the touching, and the outer; all of these are relational except the surface, so it is quantity because it is a surface. And likewise in the second definition: to the limit, and the container is also relational. So if place were a quantity because it is a limit and a container, then the thing insofar as it is relational would be of quantity, and because if it is quantity, then either being a limit or a container enters into its being quantity, in which case for place insofar as it is quantity, the quantity that benefits its being a limit and a container gives it the specificity of receiving dimensions other than what the surface gives. Or it does not, and then this whole complex is only of quantity because some part of it or its subject, which is the surface, is of quantity, so the quantity in reality is the surface.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage presents and critiques a view that place (makan) is a continuous quantity distinct from surface. The author reports that some define place as the inner surface of the containing body that touches the outer surface of the contained body, or as the limit of the encompassing body. The critique argues that these definitions mix relational terms (inner, container, touching) with the term 'surface' which alone is quantity. The relational terms are not quantitative; they are relative (al-mudaf). Therefore, if place were quantity because of these relational aspects, then relational things would be quantity — which is false. Alternatively, if only the surface aspect makes it quantity, then place is not distinct from surface. Thus the definition fails to establish place as a separate type of continuous quantity. This is a logical critique of a position, likely from the Kalām tradition about the nature of space.

Key Terms
المكان
المضاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the definition of place as a continuous quantity?
A) Because place is not extended
B) Because the definition includes relational terms that are not quantity
C) Because place is a discrete quantity
D) Because the surface alone is quantity, not the relational aspects

Key Takeaway
A definition that mixes relational terms with quantitative ones fails to establish a distinct category of quantity; the quantitative reality reduces to the genuinely quantitative component.

?

Time and Number as Continuous and Discrete Quantities
Defining time and number as quantities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus and differentia of time and number as quantities, and distinguish between continuous and discrete quantity.

Core Idea
Time is continuous quantity measured by motion; number is the essential discrete quantity composed of units.

In this opening part, the author defines time and number to establish the categories of quantity. Time is treated as a non-stable continuous quantity, while number is the paradigm of discrete quantity. Understanding these definitions is foundational for later discussions on quantity in Islamic philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the continuous quantity
Find where the author discusses time as a non-stable (غير القار) continuous quantity. Note its definition as measure of celestial motion and its limit, the 'now'.
2
Identify the discrete quantity
Find the discussion of number (العدد) as the essential discrete quantity composed of units (آحاد).
3
Define units
Note that units are either indivisible meanings or things having unity. Number is the sum of these units.
4
Distinguish essential vs accidental quantity
Observe that other things bear number accidentally; they are not essentially discrete but become quantified by having units. This sets up the later refutation of speech as discrete quantity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما غير القار وهو الزمان فهو مقدار حركة الفلك، والحد المشترك بين أجزائه المفروضة هو الآن؛ وذلك طرف الزمان على ما عرف. وأما المنفصل فذلك لا يمكن أن يكون غير العدد فإن المنفصل قوامه من متفرقات هي آحاد. والآحاد إما نفس المعنى الذي لا ينقسم من حيث هو لا ينقسم أو شيء فيه الوحدة؛ فالوحدات هي التي لذاتها يجتمع منها شيء ذو كم منفصل لذاته يكون عدد مبلغ تلك الوحدات. وأما الأمور التي فيها تلك الوحدات فهي بجملتها حاملة للعدد الذي هو لذاته كم منفصل؛ ولا يوجد فيها معنى الكمية المنفصلة إلا اجتماع تلك الآحاد ولا يوجد فيها ما يقررها سوى العدد ولا المساواة ولا اللَّا مساواة كذلك من غير أن يلتفت إلى العدد، فلا يوجد من الأشياء ما ينسب إلى احتمال تقدير ومساواة إلا وأن يعتبر فيه الاتصال أو الانفصال الذي مرَّ ذكره في العدد. وقد ذهب قوم إلى أن القول المسموع من الكم المنفصل بالذات، وذلك عند بعضهم باعتبار المقاطع التي تقدر الجملة بأزمنتها؛ وحينئذ يكون القول من الكم بحسب ما يقارنه وهو يورد أقسام الأزمنة لا لذاته. وعند بعضهم باعتبار عظم الصوت وصغره؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون الصوت بنفسه من الكم لا القول. ولأنه أي الصوت إذا كان من الكم بهذه الجهة فلا يكون من الكم المنفصل بل من المتصل . ثم إن عظم الصوت وصغره هو ثقله وخفته وجهارته وخفاءته وهي كيفيات بالحقيقة، وعند بعضهم إنه لا يكون من الكم إلا أن المقطع جزأه وهو بعده وكل ذي جزء يقدر بجزئه فهو من الكم؛ وحينئذ لا يلزم أن يكون القول كما بالذات فإنه ليس كل ما له جزء يقدر بجزئه فهو الكم بالذات بل يجوز أن تكون له حقيقة أخرى عرضت لها كمية ما إما المقدار وإما العدد. وما يكون كذلك فلا يكون من الكميات بالذات، ولا يكون داخلا في الكمية دخول النوع في الجنس، والمقطع ليس جزءًا من القول إلا أنه واحد والقول كثير . فالقول إنما له خاصة الكم من حيث الكثرة وهي العدد.
As for the non-stable, which is time, it is the measure of the motion of the sphere, and the common limit between its assumed parts is the 'now'; that is the extremity of time as known. As for the discrete, it cannot be other than number, because the discrete consists of separate units. The units are either the very meaning that is indivisible insofar as it is indivisible, or something in which there is unity. So the units are that from which, by their essences, something having discrete quantity is composed, and the number is the sum of those units. As for the things in which those units exist, they collectively bear the number which is in itself discrete quantity; and the meaning of discrete quantity does not exist in them except the aggregation of those units, and nothing establishes them except number, nor equality nor inequality likewise without reference to number. So there is nothing among things that relates to probability of estimation and equality except that connection or disconnection, which was mentioned in number, is considered. And some people held that audible speech is essentially discrete quantity. This, according to some of them, is on consideration of the syllables that measure the sentence by its times; then speech is quantity according to what accompanies it, and it introduces the divisions of time, not in itself. And according to others, on consideration of the magnitude and smallness of the voice; then it follows that the voice itself is quantity, not speech. And because the voice, if it is quantity in this respect, then it is not discrete quantity but continuous. Moreover, the magnitude and smallness of the voice are its heaviness and lightness, loudness and softness, which are in reality qualities. And according to some, it is not quantity, but the syllable is its part, and it is its dimension, and everything that has a part is measured by its part, so it is quantity. In that case, it does not follow that speech is essentially quantity, for not everything that has a part measured by its part is essentially quantity; rather, it may have another essence to which some quantity, either magnitude or number, has occurred. And what is like that is not essentially a quantity, nor does it enter into quantity as a species enters into a genus. And the syllable is not a part of speech except that it is one and speech is many. So speech only has the property of quantity from the aspect of multiplicity, which is number.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage covers two topics: first, time and number as quantities, and second, the debate on whether speech (al-qawl) is a discrete quantity. The author begins by stating that time is a continuous quantity (though non-stable) defined as the measure of celestial motion, with the 'now' as its limit. Discrete quantity is essentially number, composed of units. Then the discussion turns to speech: some argue that audible speech is essentially discrete quantity. The author refutes this by presenting two arguments: (1) If speech is considered quantity due to the time span of its syllables, then it is quantity by accident (time accompaniment), not essentially. (2) If it is due to the intensity of sound, then sound itself would be quantity, but sound intensity is a quality (loudness/softness). Moreover, sound is continuous, not discrete. Another argument: if we consider syllables as parts, then speech would be quantity because it has parts, but the author counters that not everything with parts is essentially quantity; it could have a different essence to which quantity attaches accidentally. In conclusion, speech only has the property of quantity in terms of multiplicity (the count of syllables), which is number, but that is accidental, not essential. Therefore, speech is not an essential discrete quantity.

Key Terms
الزمان
العدد
الكم المتصل
الكم المنفصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the common limit between parts of time?
A) The past
B) The present moment (the 'now')
C) The future
D) The prime mover

Key Takeaway
Time and number are foundational categories of quantity: time is continuous and non-stable, number is discrete and composed of units.

?

Refuting the View that Speech is Essentially Discrete Quantity
Refuting the claim that speech is an essential discrete quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze and reconstruct the author's arguments against considering speech as an essential discrete quantity.

Core Idea
Speech is not essentially discrete quantity; it only accidentally has quantity through the number of its syllables.

Following the establishment of number as the essential discrete quantity, the author examines and refutes several arguments claiming that audible speech is discrete quantity. He shows that speech's quantity is accidental, not essential. This lesson walks through his refutations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
Some people held that audible speech is essentially discrete quantity. Find this statement in the Arabic.
The phrase 'ذهب قوم إلى أن القول المسموع من الكم المنفصل بالذات' indicates the claim.
2
First argument: time-based
Some argue that speech is quantity due to the time of its syllables. The author responds: this makes speech quantity by accident (via time), not essentially.
3
Second argument: sound magnitude
Others argue due to the magnitude/smallness of voice. The author counters: sound magnitude is a quality (loudness/softness), not quantity, and sound is continuous, not discrete.
4
Third argument: syllables as parts
Another view: syllable is a part, and speech has parts, so it is quantity. The author responds: having parts does not make something essentially quantity; the parts may be units of something else.
The author concludes that speech only has quantity accidentally through number (the count of syllables).

Commentary
This passage covers two topics: first, time and number as quantities, and second, the debate on whether speech (al-qawl) is a discrete quantity. The author begins by stating that time is a continuous quantity (though non-stable) defined as the measure of celestial motion, with the 'now' as its limit. Discrete quantity is essentially number, composed of units. Then the discussion turns to speech: some argue that audible speech is essentially discrete quantity. The author refutes this by presenting two arguments: (1) If speech is considered quantity due to the time span of its syllables, then it is quantity by accident (time accompaniment), not essentially. (2) If it is due to the intensity of sound, then sound itself would be quantity, but sound intensity is a quality (loudness/softness). Moreover, sound is continuous, not discrete. Another argument: if we consider syllables as parts, then speech would be quantity because it has parts, but the author counters that not everything with parts is essentially quantity; it could have a different essence to which quantity attaches accidentally. In conclusion, speech only has the property of quantity in terms of multiplicity (the count of syllables), which is number, but that is accidental, not essential. Therefore, speech is not an essential discrete quantity.

Key Terms
القول المسموع
المقطع
الكم بالذات
الكم بالعرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude is the only sense in which speech has the property of quantity?
A) It is essentially continuous quantity
B) It is accidental quantity through number of syllables
C) It is essentially discrete quantity
D) It has no quantity at all

Key Takeaway
Speech is not essentially a discrete quantity; it only accidentally possesses quantity through the number of its syllables.

?

Refuting the First Two Views on Speech as Discrete Quantity
Refutes first two arguments for speech as discrete quantity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and refute two arguments that classify audible speech as a discrete quantity based on pauses in time and magnitude of sound.

Core Idea
Speech is not intrinsically quantity—temporal and sound magnitude are accidental, not essential.

Some logicians argued that audible speech is a discrete quantity because it can be measured by pauses in time or by sound magnitude. The author refutes both: the first makes quantity depend on accompaniment, the second mistakes quality for quantity. Let's examine each rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first view
Find the phrase 'باعتبار المقاطع' and note how it claims speech is quantity by measuring sentences with time pauses.
The view relies on time as a measure.
2
Identify the author's refutation of the first view
The author says 'يكون القول من الكم بحسب ما يقارنه' — speech becomes quantity by what accompanies it (time), not intrinsically.
This distinguishes intrinsic (bi al-dhat) from accidental (bi al-'arad).
3
Identify the second view
Find 'باعتبار عظم الصوت وصغره' — some base it on sound's magnitude (loudness).
This treats sound as quantity.
4
Identify the author's refutation of the second view
The author argues: if sound were quantity, it would be continuous, not discrete. Moreover, loudness/softness are qualities, not quantities.
The refutation exposes a category error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد ذهب قوم إلى أن القول المسموع من الكم المنفصل بالذات، وذلك عند بعضهم باعتبار المقاطع التي تقدر الجملة بأزمنتها؛ وحينئذ يكون القول من الكم بحسب ما يقارنه وهو يورد أقسام الأزمنة لا لذاته. وعند بعضهم باعتبار عظم الصوت وصغره؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون الصوت بنفسه من الكم لا القول. ولأنه أي الصوت إذا كان من الكم بهذه الجهة فلا يكون من الكم المنفصل بل من المتصل . ثم إن عظم الصوت وصغره هو ثقله وخفته وجهارته وخفاءته وهي كيفيات بالحقيقة، وعند بعضهم إنه لا يكون من الكم إلا أن المقطع جزأه وهو بعده وكل ذي جزء يقدر بجزئه فهو من الكم؛ وحينئذ لا يلزم أن يكون القول كما بالذات فإنه ليس كل ما له جزء يقدر بجزئه فهو الكم بالذات بل يجوز أن تكون له حقيقة أخرى عرضت لها كمية ما إما المقدار وإما العدد. وما يكون كذلك فلا يكون من الكميات بالذات، ولا يكون داخلا في الكمية دخول النوع في الجنس. والمقطع ليس جزءًا من القول إلا أنه واحد والقول كثير . فالقول إنما له خاصة الكم من حيث الكثرة وهي العدد. فإذا لم تلتفت إلى الكثرة ولا إلى الزمان الذي يساوقها ولا إلى المقادير ما يتولد عنه الصوت أو فيه لم تجد للقول كمية البتة، ولا يقال إن المقادير قد يقع عليها العدد وكونها واقعا عليها العدد لا يمنعها أن يكون في نفسها كمية فكذلك القول ؛ إذ المقادير و إن كثرت بالعدد فلها بعد الكثرة وقبلها خاصة الكمية في حد اتصالها وأحوال يلزمها ويلحقها من حيث هي متصلات لا يلتفت فيها إلى عددها مثل كونها قابلة للتجزئة، فإن قبول التجزئة لا يحملها ذات عدد وهي تحمل المساواة واللا مساواة في حد اتصالها بالتطبيق من غير التفات إلى عدد أو شيء آخر مما يلحقها.
Some people claimed that audible speech is intrinsically a discrete quantity. According to some of them, by consideration of the pauses by which the sentence is measured in its times; then speech becomes a quantity in terms of what accompanies it, not intrinsically. According to others, by consideration of the greatness and smallness of sound; then it would follow that sound itself is a quantity, not speech. And because sound, if it were a quantity in this manner, would not be a discrete quantity but a continuous one. Moreover, greatness and smallness of sound are its heaviness and lightness, loudness and softness, which are really qualities. According to others, speech is not a quantity unless the pause is its part and its distance, and everything that has a part and is measured by its part is a quantity; then it does not follow that speech is intrinsically a quantity, for not everything that has a part measured by its part is intrinsically a quantity, but it is possible that it has another reality to which a certain quantity—either magnitude or number—is accidental. And what is like that is not intrinsically a quantity, nor does it enter into quantity as species into genus. The pause is not a part of speech, except that it is singular while speech is multiple. So speech only has the property of quantity from the perspective of multiplicity, which is number. Therefore, if you do not consider multiplicity, nor the time that accompanies it, nor the magnitudes from which sound arises or in which it exists, you will find no quantity in speech at all. And it cannot be said that magnitudes may be subject to number, and being subject to number does not prevent them from being quantities in themselves, similarly for speech; for magnitudes, even if they are multiplied by number, have before and after multiplicity the property of quantity in the state of their continuity and states that accompany them from the perspective that they are continuous, without attention to their number—such as being divisible, for divisibility does not make them possess number, and they bear equality and inequality in the state of their continuity by application without attention to number or anything else that accompanies them.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of several views that classify audible speech as a discrete quantity. The author presents three positions and refutes each. First, some consider speech discrete quantity by measuring sentences in time via pauses; the author replies that this makes speech a quantity only by its temporal accompaniment, not intrinsically. Second, some base it on the magnitude of sound; the author notes that magnitude of sound is actually a quality (loudness/softness), and if sound were quantity, it would be continuous, not discrete. Third, some argue that since pauses are parts and anything with parts measured by parts is quantity, speech must be quantity. The author counters that not everything with parts is intrinsically quantity; it may have accidental quantity (like number or magnitude), but that does not make it a quantity by essence. The pause is a single unit while speech is multiple; speech's quantitative aspect is solely its multiplicity (number). Thus, without considering multiplicity, time, or sound magnitudes, speech has no quantity. The author then defends against a potential objection: magnitudes are quantities themselves despite being counted. He explains that magnitudes have intrinsic continuous quantity (divisibility, equality) independent of number, unlike speech which only has quantity through number. Thus, speech is not a discrete quantity.

Key Terms
القول المسموع
الكم المنفصل
المقطع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a reason given by the author to reject the view that speech is a discrete quantity based on sound magnitude?
A) Sound magnitude is actually a quality, not a quantity.
B) If sound were quantity, it would be continuous, not discrete.
C) Sound magnitude is measured in decibels, which are quantitative.
D) Loudness and softness are qualities.

Key Takeaway
Temporal and sound-magnitude based arguments fail to make speech an intrinsic discrete quantity; they mistake accidental accompaniments for essential nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. In a kalam debate, a scholar argues that the recited Quran is a discrete quantity because it has letters and pauses. Using the author's refutations, write a short response showing why this classification is mistaken.
?
2. Identify a modern claim that treats a quality (e.g., loudness) as a quantity. Apply the author's reasoning to expose the category error.

The Pause Argument: Parts and Intrinsic Quantity
Refutes the third view based on pauses as parts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish intrinsic from accidental quantity using the author's argument that having parts does not entail being an intrinsic quantity.

Core Idea
Having parts measured by parts does not make something intrinsically quantitative.

A third claim argues that the pause (maqta') is a part of speech, and since anything with parts measured by parts is a quantity, speech must be quantity. The author refutes this by showing that having parts does not imply intrinsic quantity; quantity may be accidental. Let's analyse this key distinction.

Guided Path
1
Locate the third view
Find 'وعند بعضهم إنه لا يكون من الكم إلا أن المقطع جزأه' — the view that the pause is a part and anything with parts measured by parts is quantity.
This view seems plausible but is flawed.
2
Identify the author's refutation
The author says 'لا يلزم أن يكون القول كما بالذات' — it does not follow that speech is intrinsically quantity. Not everything with parts is essentially quantitative; it may have another reality with accidental quantity.
The key is the distinction between bi al-dhat (intrinsic) and bi al-'arad (accidental).
3
Understand the positive point
The author adds that if something is accidentally quantitative, it does not enter the category of quantity as species into genus.
This clarifies the ontological status.

Speech's Only Quantitative Aspect: Multiplicity
Author explains speech's quantitative aspect is only multiplicity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why speech's quantitative property is only numerical multiplicity, not intrinsic continuous quantity, using the author's comparison with magnitudes.

Core Idea
Speech has quantity only through number, not through intrinsic divisibility or continuity.

After refuting the three views, the author offers a positive account: speech's only quantitative aspect is multiplicity, which is number. He contrasts this with magnitudes (like lines or surfaces) that have intrinsic continuous quantity. This lesson unpacks that comparison and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's positive claim
Find 'القول إنما له خاصة الكم من حيث الكثرة وهي العدد' — speech only has quantity from multiplicity, i.e., number.
This is the author's conclusion.
2
Understand the condition
The author says if you ignore multiplicity, time, and sound magnitudes, you find no quantity in speech at all.
Quantity is not essential; it's tied to number.
3
Analyse the comparison with magnitudes
The author defends against an objection: magnitudes are quantities themselves despite being counted. He explains that magnitudes have intrinsic continuous quantity (divisibility, equality) independent of number, unlike speech.
This clarifies the difference between continuous and discrete quantity.

Weight is a Motive Force, Not a Quantity
Author refutes that weight belongs to the category of quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the author's argument that weight is a motive force rather than a quantity and explain why the claim that weight is a quantity is invalid.

Core Idea
Weight is not a quantity but a motive force; its measurement does not make it a quantity.

This passage critically refutes the view that weight belongs to the category of quantity. The author presents the opposing claim and then dismantles it by showing that weight is fundamentally a principle of motion, not a static quantity. We will analyse the logical steps of this refutation to understand how to distinguish between a quantity and a force in Islamic natural philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
Some people claimed that weight must be a quantity because we speak of equal, half, or quarter weights. Locate this claim in the Arabic: 'وقد زعم قوم أن الثقل من الكمية وإلا لا يوجد وزن مساويا لوزن آخر أو نصفه أو ربعه'.
The claim is based on the language of measurement, which the author will challenge.
2
Extract the author's counter-claim
The author asserts that weight is not a quantity but rather 'قوة محركة إلى السفل' (a motive force downwards). Find this statement: 'فالثقل قوة محركة إلى السفل'.
This is the central thesis: weight is a force, not a quantity.
3
Follow the argument against quantity
The author explains that equality of weight is determined by resistance and motion, not by intrinsic equality. For example, when one weight cannot move another in a balance, they are considered equal; if it can move it, it is greater. This shows that weight is defined by its effect on motion.
The author uses concrete physical scenarios to argue that weight is relational and dynamic, not a static quantity.
4
Recognise the broader implications
The author concludes that weight is a motive force, and its measurement involves motion, distance, time, and resistance (which are magnitudes in bodies), but weight itself is not a quantity. Lightness is similarly a motive force upward. The same logic applies to forced inclinations.
This refutes the original claim by showing that the attributes of weight are better explained by force theory.

وقد زعم قوم أن الثقل من الكمية وإلا لا يوجد وزن مساويا لوزن آخر أو نصفه أو ربعه، غير أنه لا يكون كذلك لأن الوزن لا يكون مساويًا بذاته؛ بل الوزن مبدأ حركة إلى أسفل. فإذا كان من الوزن ما يقاوم وزنًا آخر فلا يقدر الآخر على إسالته في الميزان راسبا في نفسه؛ قيل إنه مساو له أي غير مقاوم إياه في التحريك؛ فإن قدر قيل إنه أعظم منه فإن كان يقدر على تحريك ذلك الجسم ولا يقدر على تحريك جسم من طبيعة جسم هو ضعف ذلك الجسم لا ضعف ذلك الجسم الذي على تحريكه قيل إن هذا مساو لضعفه، والآخر مساو لنصفه. ويقال أيضًا للثقل الذي يحرك في مثل الزمان ضعف المسافة إنه ضعف ذلك. وبالجملة فالثقل قوة محركة إلى السفل، والخفة قوة محركة إلى العلو . وقد يتحد الثقل ميزان معكوس في المياه ويمكن أن يتحد موازين للميول القسرية أيضًا حكمها حكم ميزان الثقل لكنه قد يكون لا في جهة الثقل ولا يلزم من ذلك أن يكون الميول القسرية كميات، والنظر في الكل إلى الحركة والمسافة والزمان والمقاومة من المقادير في الأجسام، وإلا لا يمكن أن يقدر الثقل من حيث هو قوة، ولا الخفة أيضًا.
Some people claimed that weight belongs to quantity, otherwise there would not be a weight equal to another weight, or half or quarter of it. However, it is not like that, because weight is not equal by itself; rather, weight is a principle of downward motion. When there is a weight that resists another weight, and the other cannot cause it to fall in the balance, sinking in itself, it is said to be equal to it, i.e., not resisting it in motion; if it can, it is said to be greater than it. If it can move that body but cannot move a body of a nature which is double that body—not double the body it moves—it is said that this is equal to its double, and the other is equal to its half. Also, the weight that moves in the same time double the distance is said to be double that. In summary, weight is a motive force downwards, and lightness a motive force upwards. Weight can be measured by an inverse balance in water, and it is possible to establish balances for forced inclinations as well, whose ruling is the same as the balance of weight, but it may not be in the direction of weight, and it does not follow from that that forced inclinations are quantities. The consideration in all is motion, distance, time, and resistance from the magnitudes in bodies; otherwise, weight cannot be estimated as a force, nor lightness either.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the view that weight belongs to the category of quantity. The author begins by presenting the claim: some argue that weight must be a quantity because we speak of equal weight, half weight, etc. The author refutes this by explaining that weight is not intrinsically equal; rather, it is a principle of downward motion. Equality of weight is determined by resistance or the ability to move a body. The author describes different cases: when one weight cannot move another, it is considered equal; if it can move it, it is greater. Furthermore, weight is measured by the distance moved in a given time (e.g., double weight moves double distance in same time). Hence, weight is fundamentally a motive force (قوة محركة), not a quantity. Lightness is similarly a motive force upward. The author then notes that weight can be measured via inverse balance in water, and forced inclinations can also be measured, but that does not make them quantities. Ultimately, the measurement of weight involves motion, distance, time, and resistance (which are magnitudes in bodies), but weight itself is a force, not a quantity. Therefore, the argument that weight is a quantity is invalid.

Distinguishing Weight as a Force from Quantity
Refutation of weight as quantity by distinguishing force from magnitude

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical basis for distinguishing weight as a force from the category of quantity, using the author's argument from physical effects.

Core Idea
Weight is a force, not a quantity; its comparisons rely on motion and resistance.

In Aristotelian logic, categories distinguish types of existence. Some have claimed that weight is a quantity because we speak of equal weights, half, etc. The author refutes this by showing that weight is a principle of motion—a force—and its numerical relations are indirect, based on physical effects. Understanding this refutation sharpens your ability to critically evaluate category assignments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim under attack
Find the claim that weight is a quantity. The author quotes opponents who argue that otherwise we wouldn't speak of equal, half, or quarter weights.
The claim is that weight belongs to the category of quantity (al-kammiyya).
2
Locate the author's counter-argument
The author states that weight is 'a principle of motion downward' (mabda' haraka ila asfal), i.e., a force. Note that equality of weights is not intrinsic but based on resistance in the balance.
Key phrase: 'al-wazn la yakun musawiyan bi-dhatihi' (weight is not equal by itself).
3
Extract the criteria for weight comparison
The author explains how we determine equality, half, double: by resistance in the scale, by the ability to move bodies of different sizes, and by the distance moved in a given time. These involve motion, time, and resistance.
Weight is measured through its effects, not as a direct magnitude.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that weight is a force (quwwa muharrika) downward, and lightness is a force upward. Measurement depends on motion, distance, time, and resistance from the magnitudes in bodies.
Thus weight is not a quantity but a force, whose magnitude is inferred from its effects.
5
Reflect on the logical move
The author refutes by showing that the attributes used to claim weight as quantity (equality, half, etc.) are not intrinsic but relational and dependent on physical context. This is a common logical strategy: showing that apparent quantitative properties are actually derivative.
This aligns with the broader critique of reducing dynamic properties to static categories.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد زعم قوم أن الثقل من الكمية وإلا لا يوجد وزن مساويا لوزن آخر أو نصفه أو ربعه، غير أنه لا يكون كذلك لأن الوزن لا يكون مساويًا بذاته؛ بل الوزن مبدأ حركة إلى أسفل. فإذا كان من الوزن ما يقاوم وزنًا آخر فلا يقدر الآخر على إسالته في الميزان راسبا في نفسه؛ قيل إنه مساو له أي غير مقاوم إياه في التحريك؛ فإن قدر قيل إنه أعظم منه فإن كان يقدر على تحريك ذلك الجسم ولا يقدر على تحريك جسم من طبيعة جسم هو ضعف ذلك الجسم لا ضعف ذلك الجسم الذي على تحريكه قيل إن هذا مساو لضعفه، والآخر مساو لنصفه. ويقال أيضًا للثقل الذي يحرك في مثل الزمان ضعف المسافة إنه ضعف ذلك. وبالجملة فالثقل قوة محركة إلى السفل، والخفة قوة محركة إلى العلو . وقد يتحد الثقل ميزان معكوس في المياه ويمكن أن يتحد موازين للميول القسرية أيضًا حكمها حكم ميزان الثقل لكنه قد يكون لا في جهة الثقل ولا يلزم من ذلك أن يكون الميول القسرية كميات، والنظر في الكل إلى الحركة والمسافة والزمان والمقاومة من المقادير في الأجسام، وإلا لا يمكن أن يقدر الثقل من حيث هو قوة، ولا الخفة أيضًا.
Some people have claimed that weight is of quantity, otherwise there would not exist a weight equal to another weight or half of it or a quarter of it, but it is not so because weight is not equal by itself; rather weight is a principle of motion downward. So if there is a weight that resists another weight so that the other cannot cause it to fall in the balance (scale) sinking into itself, it is said to be equal to it, i.e., not resisting it in causing motion; if it overcomes, it is said to be greater than it. If it is capable of moving that body and cannot move a body of a nature that is double that body (not double that body which it moves), it is said that this is equal to its double, and the other is equal to its half. Also, for the weight that moves in the same time twice the distance, it is said to be double that. In summary, weight is a force moving downward, and lightness is a force moving upward. And weight may be measured by an inverse balance in waters, and balances for forced inclinations may also have the same ruling as the balance of weight, but it may not be in the direction of weight, and it does not follow from that that forced inclinations are quantities. The consideration in all these is to motion, distance, time, and resistance from the magnitudes in bodies; otherwise it is not possible to measure weight as a force, nor lightness either.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the claim that weight (al-thiqal) belongs to the category of quantity. The author argues that weight is not a quantity in itself, despite the fact that we speak of weights being equal, half, or double. The author explains that weight is a principle of motion downward (mabda' haraka ila asfal), i.e., a force (quwwa). The equality of weights is not a direct equality of their essences but rather based on their effects in the balance (mizan). When two weights resist each other such that neither overcomes the other, we say they are equal. When one overcomes, we say it is greater. Additionally, if a weight moves a body in a certain time but cannot move a body twice as heavy, it is considered equal to half of that heavier body. Similarly, moving the same distance in half the time indicates double the weight. The author concludes that weight and its opposite, lightness (al-khiffa), are forces (quwwa muharrika) — one downward, one upward. The measurement of these forces involves motion, distance, time, and resistance, which are themselves magnitudes (maqadir) in bodies. Thus, weight is not a quantity, but a force measured by its effects. This refutation is important for understanding the categories (al-maqulat) in Aristotelian logic and how they apply to physical phenomena.

The Homonymy of Position (Wad') in Quantity
Author presents the second division of quantity into those with position and those without, and defines position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three meanings of 'wad'' (position) and identify which sense applies in the division of quantity.

Core Idea
Position in quantity is not sitting or standing—it is the stable arrangement of parts that can be pointed to.

In this passage, the author explores a key distinction in the category of quantity: some quantities have position in their parts, others do not. But the term 'position' is ambiguous. Understanding this ambiguity is crucial for avoiding confusion when reading later logical and metaphysical texts, especially when the same term appears in discussions of categories.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of quantity
Find the phrases 'ما له وضع' and 'ما ليس له وضع' in the Arabic. The author distinguishes quantities based on whether their parts have position relative to each other.
For example, a line has position (parts are arranged spatially), but time does not.
2
Extract the definition of having position
The author states that parts with position must have stable existence (wujud qarr) together, so that there is contact and arrangement that allows pointing (ishara). This is the condition for 'position' in quantity.
Stable existence means the parts coexist actually, not just potentially.
3
List the three meanings of 'wad''
The author enumerates: (1) anything that can be pointed to (e.g., a point), (2) the more specific meaning used for quantities with position, (3) the category of position among the nine accidents (e.g., sitting, standing).
The third meaning applies truly only to substances.
4
Understand the transfer of meaning
The author explains that the position used in quantity is transferred (manqul) from the categorical sense, because of the relevance of parts being relative to each other.
This transfer is a common phenomenon in Arabic terminology.
5
Evaluate the objection about moving bodies
The author addresses the claim that a moving body has no position. He argues that motion does not remove the kind of position relevant to quantity, only the stable categorical position. The distinction is like having 'where' (ayn) vs. having stable 'where'.
This shows the importance of specifying which sense of 'position' is meant.
