فصل : [ في القسمة الأخرى للكمية ) وأما القسمة الأخرى للكمية فهي أن الكمية منها ما له وضع في أجزائه، ومنها ما ليس له وضع في أجزائه، والأجزاء التي لها وضع يجب أن يكون لها وجود قار بالفعل معا ليكون لبعضها عند بعض اتصال وترصيف يوقعانه تحت الإشارة أن كل واحد منها أين هو من صاحبه. والوضع اسم مشترك يقع على معانٍ شتى فيقال وضع لكل ما إليه إشارة كيف كانت والإشارة هي تعيين الجهة التي يختص الشيء من جهات العالم بهذا المعنى يقال إن للنقطة وضعا وليس للوحدة وضع. ويقال وضع للمعنى أخص من هذا كما إذا قلت إن لبعض / الكميات وضعًا كما مرَّ ويقال وضع للمعنى الذي يشتمل عليه [٥٣] مقولة من المقولات التسع كما ستعرفه. وهذا الوضع لا يقال قولا حقيقيًّا إلا على الجواهر، وقد يقال وضع لمعنى أخرى لا يتعلق بالمقادير ولا بالإشارة والوضع الذي هو في باب الكم كأنه نقل من الوضع الذي هو من المقولات اسمه لما أنه يناسب ذلك في اعتبار حال الأجزاء بعضها عند البعض. وقد قيل إن الجسم المتحرك لا وضع له وفيه نظر، فإنه إذا عني به الوضع الذي هو من المقولة يوهم أنه صدق لكن ليس كذلك؛ إذ الفرق بين بين أن يكون للشيء وضع وبين أن لا يكون له وضع قار كما هو بين بين أن لا يكون للجسم أين وبين أن لا يكون له أين قار. وكما أن الحركة عند التحقيق لا يُخرج الجسم عن أن يكون ذا أين وإن أخرجه عن أن يكون ذا أين قار فكذلك الحركة بالقياس إلى الوضع لكن الوضع الذي يعتبر في مقولة الكم غير ذلك الوضع وهو غير معتبر في الجسم المتحرك وإن تحرك. فإن الحركة لا تعدم شيئًا من شرائط هذا الوضع الذي هو كون الكمية بحيث يمكن أن يفرض فيها أجزاء إذا
Section: On the Other Division of Quantity. As for the other division of quantity, it is that quantity includes that which has position (wad') in its parts, and that which does not have position in its parts. The parts that have position must have a stable existence (wujud qarr) actually together so that there is for some of them next to others a contact and arrangement that brings them under indication (ishara), so that each one of them is 'where' it is relative to its companion. Position is a homonym (ism mushtarak) applying to various meanings. It is said 'position' for everything that can be pointed to, however that may be. Indication is the determination of the direction that a thing occupies among the directions of the world. In this sense, it is said that the point has position, but the unit (wahda) does not have position. And 'position' is said for a meaning more specific than this, as when you say that some quantities have position as previously mentioned. And 'position' is said for the meaning encompassed by the category (maqula) of position among the nine categories, as you will learn. This position is not said truly except of substances. And 'position' may be said for other meanings not related to magnitudes nor to indication. The position that is in the chapter of quantity seems to be transferred from the position that is one of the categories, because it is appropriate to that in considering the state of parts relative to each other. It has been said that the moving body has no position, but there is consideration (nazar) in this. For if what is meant is the position that is one of the categories, it might be thought that it is true, but it is not so. For the difference between a thing having position and not having stable (qarr) position is as clear as the difference between a body having 'where' (ayn) and not having stable 'where'. Just as motion, upon investigation, does not remove the body from being a possessor of 'where', although it removes it from being a possessor of stable 'where', so too motion in relation to position. But the position considered in the category of quantity is different from that position, and it is not considered in the moving body even if it moves. For motion does not remove any of the conditions of this position, which is that the quantity be such that parts can be supposed in it if...
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition of the second division of quantity (al-kammiyya). The author states that quantity is divided into two types: one whose parts have position (wad') relative to each other, and one whose parts do not. The first type includes, for example, line, surface, body; the second includes number and time. The author explains that parts with position must exist together in a stable manner (wujud qarr bi'l-fi'l), so that they can be pointed to (ishara) indicating where each part is relative to the others. The term 'position' (wad') is a homonym (ism mushtarak), having multiple senses. The first sense: anything that can be pointed to, even a point (which has position but no magnitude). The second sense: the more specific meaning used for quantities that have position (as in the first division). The third sense: the category (maqula) of position among the nine accidental categories (e.g., sitting, standing). This third sense applies truly only to substances. The author notes that the term 'position' as used in the discussion of quantity seems to be transferred (manqul) from the categorical sense, because of the relevance of considering parts relative to each other. Then the author addresses an objection: it is said that a moving body has no position. The author examines this: if by 'position' we mean the categorical sense, then it might seem true, but it is not, because the difference between having position and having stable position is analogous to having 'where' (ayn) and having stable 'where'. Motion does not remove the body from having an 'where', even if it removes stable 'where'. Similarly, motion does not remove the position relevant to quantity, which is simply the possibility of supposing parts arranged in space. Motion does not destroy this condition. The passage is incomplete in this window, but the discussion continues.

The Second Division of Quantity: With and Without Position
Author divides quantity into with and without position, defines wad' as homonym.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the term 'wad'' (position) as used in the division of quantity, distinguish its homonymous meanings, and determine whether a given quantity has position based on the condition of simultaneous coexistence and arrangement of parts.

Core Idea
Position in quantity means parts that coexist and are arranged so that each can be pointed to relative to the other.

This passage presents the second division of quantity: some quantities have position in their parts, others do not. But what exactly does 'position' mean here? The author explains that the term 'wad'' is a homonym with several meanings, and he specifies which one applies to quantity. By understanding this definition, you will be able to correctly classify quantities like line, time, or number.

Guided Path
1
Locate the division
Read the opening sentence: 'As for the other division of quantity, it is that quantity includes that which has position in its parts, and that which does not have position in its parts.' Identify the two categories.
2
Define the condition for position
The author states that parts with position must have 'fixed existence in act simultaneously' and be arranged so that each part can be indicated relative to another. Write this condition in your own words.
This means the parts must exist at the same time and be connected in space.
3
Recognize wad' as a homonym
The passage lists several meanings of 'wad'': (1) anything that can be pointed to, (2) a specific meaning for quantity where parts are arranged, (3) the category of position among the nine categories, and (4) other unrelated meanings. List them.
The relevant meaning for quantity is the second one.
4
Explain the transfer of meaning
The author says the quantitative wad' is 'transferred' from the categorical wad'. Understand that it resembles the category of position but is applied specifically to parts of a quantity.
This avoids confusion with the category itself.
5
Test examples
Apply the definition to line, time, number, and speech. Does each have parts that coexist and are arranged? Line: yes. Time: no (parts do not coexist). Number: no (parts have order but no connection). Speech: no (no natural order).
This will prepare you for the next lesson's enumeration.

فصل : في القسمة الأخرى للكمية

وأما القسمة الأخرى للكمية فهي أن الكمية منها ما له وضع في أجزائه، ومنها ما ليس له وضع في أجزائه، والأجزاء التي لها وضع يجب أن يكون لها وجود قار بالفعل معا ليكون لبعضها عند بعض اتصال وترصيف يوقعانه تحت الإشارة أن كل واحد منها أين هو من صاحبه. والوضع اسم مشترك يقع على معانٍ شتى فيقال وضع لكل ما إليه إشارة كيف كانت والإشارة هي تعيين الجهة التي يختص الشيء من جهات العالم بهذا المعنى يقال إن للنقطة وضعا وليس للوحدة وضع. ويقال وضع للمعنى أخص من هذا كما إذا قلت إن لبعض الكميات وضعًا كما مرَّ ويقال وضع للمعنى الذي يشتمل عليه مقولة من المقولات التسع كما ستعرفه. وهذا الوضع لا يقال قولا حقيقيًّا إلا على الجواهر، وقد يقال وضع لمعنى أخرى لا يتعلق بالمقادير ولا بالإشارة والوضع الذي هو في باب الكم كأنه نقل من الوضع الذي هو من المقولات اسمه لما أنه يناسب ذلك في اعتبار حال الأجزاء بعضها عند البعض.

وقد قيل إن الجسم المتحرك لا وضع له وفيه نظر، فإنه إذا عني به الوضع الذي هو من المقولة يوهم أنه صدق لكن ليس كذلك؛ إذ الفرق بين بين أن يكون للشيء وضع وبين أن لا يكون له وضع قار كما هو بين بين أن لا يكون للجسم أين وبين أن لا يكون له أين قار. وكما أن الحركة عند التحقيق لا تُخرج الجسم عن أن يكون ذا أين وإن أخرجه عن أن يكون ذا أين قار فكذلك الحركة بالقياس إلى الوضع لكن الوضع الذي يعتبر في مقولة الكم غير ذلك الوضع وهو غير معتبر في الجسم المتحرك وإن تحرك. فإن الحركة لا تعدم شيئًا من شرائط هذا الوضع الذي هو كون الكمية بحيث يمكن أن يفرض فيها أجزاء إذا فرضت تكون متصلة ويكون بعضها تحت بعض يلزم ذلك التحت في الجسم. فإن الحركة لا تزيل المجاورة بينها ولا تمنع عن أن تشار إلى كل جزأين هو من الآخر. فتلك المجاورة محفوظة وإن كانت متبدلة مع أشياء خارجة لكنه وإن كان كذلك فإنها توجب النقل عن وضع إلى وضع وهذا لا يوجب أن يكون المنقول لا وضع له بل يوجب أن الوضع متبدل على الاتصال وعلى الانفصال.

ثم إن كان قسمة الكم أي ذي وضع وغير ذي وضع قسمة بالفصول لم تجز أن تبدل الفصول بحركة ويكون بعرض الجسم. ولا خفاء في أن الخط لأجزائه وضع. وكذلك السطح والجسم والمكان كذلك؛ فإن لأجزائه وضعا بالقياس إلى ما هو سطحه وبالقياس إلى ما هو حاويه. وأما الزمان فلا يكون لأجزائه وضع؛ إذ لا يوجد جزء منها مقارناً للجزء، وإن كانت توجد في أجزائه اتصال وترتيب من جهة التقدم والتأخر. وأما العدد فإنه وإن وجد لأجزائه تقرر وترتيب فلا يوجد لها اتصال فلا يكون له وضع لكن يعرض له أن يصير ذا وضع بسبب ما يقارنه. والقول أبعد من ذلك فإنه لا يكون على ترتيب طبيعي. والكم ذو الوضع هو المقدار والمقادير بالحقيقة ثلاثة؛ وإذا أخذ فيها المكان صارت أربعة. فالزمان والعدد والقول غير ذوات وضع؛ فهذه هي الكميات بالحقيقة. وقد يكون من الأشياء ما يقال إنه من الكميات ويكون ذلك بالعرض لا بالذات لما أنه سبب المقارنة لكميات هي بالذات. وذلك إما من الموضوعات لها كالإنسان والفرس فيقال إنه قصير وطويل، وإما من الأعراض التي لا توجد إلا مع الكميات كالحركة فإنها لا توجد إلا بمقارنة من جسم متحرك لما فيه تكون الحركة فيها. والزمان تكون هي أيضًا فيه فتقدر به فيقال حركة طويلة أي في مسافة طويلة أو في زمان طويل، ويقال أيضًا بياض عريض أي في سطح عريض. وقد يكون من هذه العوارض ما يكون خاصة
Chapter: On the Other Division of Quantity

As for the other division of quantity, it is that quantity includes that which has position in its parts, and that which does not have position in its parts. The parts that have position must have a fixed existence in act simultaneously so that there is between some of them with respect to others connection and arrangement that places them under indication, that each one of them is where it is from its companion. And position (wad') is a homonym that falls on various meanings. So 'wad'' is said for everything to which there is indication, however it may be. And indication is the specification of the direction that the thing is distinguished from the directions of the world. In this sense, it is said that the point has a position, and the unit does not have a position. And 'wad'' is said for a meaning more specific than this, as when you say that some quantities have position as has passed. And 'wad'' is said for the meaning that encompasses one of the nine categories, as you will come to know. This wad' is not said in a true sense except for substances. And 'wad'' may be said for other meanings not related to magnitudes nor to indication. The wad' that is in the chapter of quantity is as if transferred from the wad' that is one of the categories, because it resembles that in considering the state of parts with respect to one another.

It has been said that the moving body has no position, but there is a consideration in this. For if what is meant by it is the wad' that is from the category, it may be supposed that it is true, but it is not so. For the difference between something having a position and not having a fixed position is clear, just as it is clear between a body not having a 'where' and not having a fixed 'where'. And just as motion, upon investigation, does not remove the body from having a 'where', even if it removes it from having a fixed 'where', so also motion with respect to position. But the wad' that is considered in the category of quantity is other than that wad', and it is not considered in the moving body even if it moves. For motion does not negate any of the conditions of this wad', which is that quantity is such that it is possible to assume in it parts that, when assumed, are continuous and some are under others, necessitating that subordination in the body. For motion does not remove the adjacency between them, nor does it prevent indicating each two parts where one is from the other. That adjacency is preserved, even if it changes with external things. But even if it is so, it necessitates transfer from one position to another, and this does not require that the transferred thing has no position; rather it requires that the position changes continuously and discontinuously.

Then if the division of quantity into that which has position and that which does not have position is a division by differentiate, it is not permissible that the differentiate change by motion and be accidental to the body. And it is not hidden that line has position for its parts, and similarly surface, body, and place likewise. For each has position for its parts in relation to what is its surface and in relation to what contains it. As for time, its parts have no position, because no part of it exists simultaneously with another part, even if there exists in its parts connection and order in terms of priority and posteriority. As for number, even if there exists for its parts determination and order, there is no connection for them, so it has no position, but it can accidentally become having position due to what it accompanies. Speech is further from that, for it has no natural order. The quantity that has position is magnitude (miqdar), and the magnitudes in reality are three; when place is taken in them, they become four. So time, number, and speech are not having position; these are the quantities in reality. There may be things that are said to be among quantities and that is accidentally, not essentially, because they are the cause of accompanying quantities that are essential. That is either from the subjects of them, like man and horse, so it is said that he is short or tall; or from the accidents that exist only with quantities, like motion, for it exists only by accompanying a moving body in which motion occurs, and time is also in it, so it is measured by it, so it is said 'long motion', meaning in a long distance or in a long time, and it is also said 'wide whiteness', meaning on a wide surface. And there may be from these accidents what is particular...
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory passage on the second division of quantity (al-kammiyya). The author begins by stating the division: quantity either has position (wad') in its parts or does not. He then explains that for parts to have position, they must have simultaneous fixed existence and be arranged such that each part can be indicated relative to another. The term 'wad'' is a homonym (mushtarak) with multiple meanings: (1) anything that can be pointed to, (2) a more specific meaning related to quantity (as in the division), (3) the category of position (one of the nine categories), and (4) other unrelated meanings. The wad' relevant to quantity is said to be transferred from the categorical wad' because it resembles it in considering the arrangement of parts.

The author then addresses an objection: some claim a moving body has no position. He refines this: if by 'position' they mean the category of position, they might think it true but it is not; the distinction is between having a position and having a fixed position. Motion removes fixed position but not position in the quantitative sense—the adjacency of parts is preserved. Thus, a moving body still has quantitative position, though it changes.

Finally, the author enumerates which quantities have position and which do not: line, surface, body, and place have position; time, number, and speech do not. The intrinsic quantities with position are magnitudes (miqdar), essentially three (line, surface, body) and four if place is included. Time, number, and speech are quantities without position. He then discusses accidental quantities: things like tall/short humans (subjects) or motion/whiteness (accidents) that are measured by true quantities.

For example, a line (khatt) such as the edge of a prayer mat has continuous parts with position; time (zaman) such as the hours of prayer has parts that do not coexist, so no position. Number ('adad) like the number of rak'at has order but no connection. Speech (qawl) like a sentence has no natural order. Accidental quantities include a tall man (height is a quantity by subject) or a long motion (motion measured by time or distance).

The passage is crucial for understanding the Aristotelian category of quantity and the concept of wad' in Islamic logic.

C) The category of position among the nine categories

Key Takeaway
Position (wad') in quantity is defined by the simultaneous existence and arrangement of parts that allow relative indication; it is one of several homonymous meanings of the term.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Ten Categories
intermediate
12 min
Quantities With and Without Position: Real and Accidental
Author enumerates quantities with/without position and discusses accidental quantities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate which quantities essentially have position (magnitudes) and which do not (time, number, speech), and distinguish essential from accidental quantitative attributes.

Core Idea
Only magnitudes (line, surface, body, place) have essential position; time, number, and speech lack position, but many things are called quantities accidentally.

After defining position, the author lists which quantities have it and which do not. He also addresses a potential objection: does a moving body lose position? He clarifies that motion changes position but does not remove quantitative position. Finally, he distinguishes between quantities that are essentially quantitative and those that are accidentally described as such, like a tall man or a long motion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quantities with position
The passage states that line, surface, body, and place have position. List them and note that they are called magnitudes (miqdar).
Place is said to become a fourth magnitude when taken in relation to surfaces.
2
Identify the quantities without position
Time, number, and speech are said not to have position. For each, explain why: time's parts do not coexist; number has order but no connection; speech has no natural order.
These are the essential quantities without position.
3
Analyze the moving body objection
The author refutes the claim that a moving body has no position. He distinguishes between having a position and having a fixed position. Motion negates fixed position but not quantitative position, because adjacency of parts is preserved.
This clarification prevents a misunderstanding of the division.
4
Understand accidental quantities
The passage presents two ways something can be accidentally quantitative: by being a subject that possesses a quantity (e.g., man is tall) or by being an accident that accompanies a quantity (e.g., motion measured by time or distance).
Examples: 'long motion' means long in time or distance; 'wide whiteness' means on a wide surface.
5
Practice classification
Take each of the following and classify as essential quantity with position, essential quantity without position, accidental by subject, or accidental by accompanying quantity: the number of rak'at, the height of a mountain, the duration of a prayer, a square piece of cloth.
This step consolidates the entire classification scheme.

Chapter: On the Other Division of Quantity

As for the other division of quantity, it is that quantity includes that which has position in its parts, and that which does not have position in its parts. The parts that have position must have a fixed existence in act simultaneously so that there is between some of them with respect to others connection and arrangement that places them under indication, that each one of them is where it is from its companion. And position (wad') is a homonym that falls on various meanings. So 'wad'' is said for everything to which there is indication, however it may be. And indication is the specification of the direction that the thing is distinguished from the directions of the world. In this sense, it is said that the point has a position, and the unit does not have a position. And 'wad'' is said for a meaning more specific than this, as when you say that some quantities have position as has passed. And 'wad'' is said for the meaning that encompasses one of the nine categories, as you will come to know. This wad' is not said in a true sense except for substances. And 'wad'' may be said for other meanings not related to magnitudes nor to indication. The wad' that is in the chapter of quantity is as if transferred from the wad' that is one of the categories, because it resembles that in considering the state of parts with respect to one another.

It has been said that the moving body has no position, but there is a consideration in this. For if what is meant by it is the wad' that is from the category, it may be supposed that it is true, but it is not so. For the difference between something having a position and not having a fixed position is clear, just as it is clear between a body not having a 'where' and not having a fixed 'where'. And just as motion, upon investigation, does not remove the body from having a 'where', even if it removes it from having a fixed 'where', so also motion with respect to position. But the wad' that is considered in the category of quantity is other than that wad', and it is not considered in the moving body even if it moves. For motion does not negate any of the conditions of this wad', which is that quantity is such that it is possible to assume in it parts that, when assumed, are continuous and some are under others, necessitating that subordination in the body. For motion does not remove the adjacency between them, nor does it prevent indicating each two parts where one is from the other. That adjacency is preserved, even if it changes with external things. But even if it is so, it necessitates transfer from one position to another, and this does not require that the transferred thing has no position; rather it requires that the position changes continuously and discontinuously.

Then if the division of quantity into that which has position and that which does not have position is a division by differentiate, it is not permissible that the differentiate change by motion and be accidental to the body. And it is not hidden that line has position for its parts, and similarly surface, body, and place likewise. For each has position for its parts in relation to what is its surface and in relation to what contains it. As for time, its parts have no position, because no part of it exists simultaneously with another part, even if there exists in its parts connection and order in terms of priority and posteriority. As for number, even if there exists for its parts determination and order, there is no connection for them, so it has no position, but it can accidentally become having position due to what it accompanies. Speech is further from that, for it has no natural order. The quantity that has position is magnitude (miqdar), and the magnitudes in reality are three; when place is taken in them, they become four. So time, number, and speech are not having position; these are the quantities in reality. There may be things that are said to be among quantities and that is accidentally, not essentially, because they are the cause of accompanying quantities that are essential. That is either from the subjects of them, like man and horse, so it is said that he is short or tall; or from the accidents that exist only with quantities, like motion, for it exists only by accompanying a moving body in which motion occurs, and time is also in it, so it is measured by it, so it is said 'long motion', meaning in a long distance or in a long time, and it is also said 'wide whiteness', meaning on a wide surface. And there may be from these accidents what is particular...
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory passage on the second division of quantity (al-kammiyya). The author begins by stating the division: quantity either has position (wad') in its parts or does not. He then explains that for parts to have position, they must have simultaneous fixed existence and be arranged such that each part can be indicated relative to another. The term 'wad'' is a homonym (mushtarak) with multiple meanings: (1) anything that can be pointed to, (2) a more specific meaning related to quantity (as in the division), (3) the category of position (one of the nine categories), and (4) other unrelated meanings. The wad' relevant to quantity is said to be transferred from the categorical wad' because it resembles it in considering the arrangement of parts.

The author then addresses an objection: some claim a moving body has no position. He refines this: if by 'position' they mean the category of position, they might think it true but it is not; the distinction is between having a position and having a fixed position. Motion removes fixed position but not position in the quantitative sense—the adjacency of parts is preserved. Thus, a moving body still has quantitative position, though it changes.

Finally, the author enumerates which quantities have position and which do not: line, surface, body, and place have position; time, number, and speech do not. The intrinsic quantities with position are magnitudes (miqdar), essentially three (line, surface, body) and four if place is included. Time, number, and speech are quantities without position. He then discusses accidental quantities: things like tall/short humans (subjects) or motion/whiteness (accidents) that are measured by true quantities.

For example, a line (khatt) such as the edge of a prayer mat has continuous parts with position; time (zaman) such as the hours of prayer has parts that do not coexist, so no position. Number ('adad) like the number of rak'at has order but no connection. Speech (qawl) like a sentence has no natural order. Accidental quantities include a tall man (height is a quantity by subject) or a long motion (motion measured by time or distance).

The passage is crucial for understanding the Aristotelian category of quantity and the concept of wad' in Islamic logic.

Key Takeaway
The essential quantities with position are the three magnitudes (line, surface, body) and place; time, number, and speech are without position; many things are accidentally quantitative by being subjects or accompaniments of quantities.

Justify each using the passage's criteria.

Explain why the passage says 'the moving body has no position (in the categorical sense) but has quantitative position.' Provide an example from daily life.

Essential Division of Quantity: With and Without Position
Author differentiates essential quantities by spatial co-existence of parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify essential quantities as dhu wad' or ghayr dhi wad' based on the criteria of co-existence and continuity.

Core Idea
Position requires parts that coexist in space; time and number fail this test.

In this lesson, we study the foundational division of quantity into two categories: those whose parts have a spatial position relative to each other, and those whose parts do not. The author provides clear criteria and examples from geometry and physics.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two classes
Read the passage to find the names of the two divisions: dhu wad' (with position) and ghayr dhi wad' (without position).
These are the primary categories under discussion.
2
Determine the criteria for having position
Notice that the author implies that for a quantity to have position, its parts must co-exist and have a spatial relation. For time, he says parts do not co-exist; for number, parts lack continuity.
Co-existence and continuity are key.
3
Apply the criteria to examples
Classify line, surface, body, and place as having position because their parts coexist. Classify time, number, and speech as lacking position because they fail the criteria.
Use the author's own reasoning.
4
Identify the number of real magnitudes
The author states that magnitude (al-miqdar) is the quantity with position, and there are three primary magnitudes: line, surface, body. Place makes a fourth if included.
This is the essential classification.

تبدل الفصول بحركة ويكون بعرض الجسم. ولا خفاء في أن الخط لأجزائه وضع. وكذلك السطح والجسم والمكان كذلك؛ فإن لأجزائه وضعا بالقياس إلى ما هو سطحه وبالقياس إلى ما هو حاويه. وأما الزمان فلا يكون لأجزائه الكميات بالحقيقة. وقد يكون من الأشياء ما يقال إنه من الكميات ويكون ذلك بالعرض لا بالذات لما أنه سبب المقارنة لكميات هي بالذات. وذلك إما من الموضوعات لها كالإنسان والفرس فيقال إنه قصير وطويل، وإما من الأعراض التي لا توجد إلا مع الكميات كالحركة فإنها لا توجد إلا بمقارنة من جسم متحرك لما فيه تكون الحركة فيها. والزمان تكون هي أيضًا فيه فتقدر به فيقال حركة طويلة أي في مسافة طويلة أو في زمان طويل، ويقال أيضًا بياض عريض أي في سطح عريض. وقد يكون من هذه العوارض ما يكون خاصة للكمية كالطول والقصر الذي بالقياس مثل ما يقال إن هذا الخط طويل والآخر قصير وإن كان كل خط طويلا في نفسه، وهذا السطح عريض والآخر ضيق وإن كان كل سطح عريضًا في نفسه، وهذا الجسم ثخين والآخر رقيق وإن كان كل جسم ثخينًا في نفسه، وهذا العدد كثير والآخر قليل وإن كان كل عدد كثيرًا في نفسه على ما عرف؛ فهذه وأمثالها أحوال تعرض للكم بمقايسة البعض منها إلى البعض. ثم الطول يقال باشتراك الاسم على معان على امتداد واحد كيف كان وعلى الامتداد الذي يعرض أولاً، وعلى الطول لامتدادين، وعلى الامتداد الواحد من مركز العالم إلى محيطه، وعلى طول الإنسان وهو البعد الذي فيه أول حركته. وكذلك العرض يقال للكم الذي فيه بعدان، وللبعد الواحد الذي يعرض مقاطعًا لبعد فرض أولا؛ ولأقصر البعدين المتقاطعين، وللبعد الواحد من اليمين والشمال والعمق أيضًا يقال للشخن المحصور في السطوح وللبعد المقاطع للبعدين المفروضين أولا ولما يحويه قدام الإنسان وخلفه وفي الغير من الحيوانات أعلاه وأسفله. ثم النقطة إذا تحركت أو فرضت متحركة في جهة ما ارتسم طول و خط؛ فإذا تحرك هذا الخط لا في جهة حركة النقطة بل في جهة مقاطعة لها ارتسم سطح وعرض؛ فإن ارتفع السطح أو انخفض حتى يكون حركة على بعد مقاطع للبعدين على قوائم ارتسم جسم وظاهر للجسم من حيث هو ظاهره فهو بسيط وسطح. فإن قطع هذا السطح فالطرف الحاصل بالقطع فقط هو الخط؛ وإن قطع الخط فطرفه النقطة. ثم الخط المحدود هو البعد الذي يفرض بين نقطتين، والسطح المحدود هو البعد الذي يفرض بين خطين، والعمق المحدود هو البعد الذي يفرض بين سطحين. ثم الطول والعرض والعمق بدون الإضافة من الكميات ومع الإضافة من الأعراض
Supposed to be continuous and some under some, that entails substructure in body. For motion does not remove contiguity between them nor prevent pointing to each two parts one from the other. So that contiguity is preserved even if it changes with external things, but although it is so, it necessitates transfer from position to position, and this does not necessitate that the transferred thing has no position, but rather necessitates that the position changes continuously and discretely. Then if the division of quantity into that with position and that without position is a division by differentiae, it is not permissible that the differentiae be changed by motion and be an accident of body. And it is not hidden that the line has position for its parts, and likewise the surface and the body and place; for their parts have position relative to what is its surface and relative to what encompasses it. As for time, it has no position for its parts, since no part of it exists concomitant with another part, even though there is in its parts continuity and order in terms of priority and posteriority. As for number, even though its parts have determinacy and order, they have no continuity, so it has no position, but it may become possessed of position due to what accompanies it. And speech is further from that, for it is not in a natural order. Quantity with position is magnitude, and magnitudes in reality are three; if place is included in them, they become four. Time, number, and speech are without position; these are the quantities in reality. And there are things that are said to be quantities accidentally, not essentially, because they are the cause of comparison with quantities essentially. That either from the subjects of them, like man and horse, so it is said short and tall, or from accidents that do not exist except with quantities, like motion, for it does not exist except with the comparison of a moving body to what motion occurs in it. And time also exists in it, so it is estimated by it, so it is said long motion, i.e., in a long distance or in a long time, and it is also said wide whiteness, i.e., on a wide surface. And among these accidents there may be what is specific to quantity, like length and shortness relatively, as it is said this line is long and that one is short even though every line is long in itself, and this surface is wide and that one narrow even though every surface is wide in itself, and this body is thick and that one thin even though every body is thick in itself, and this number is many and that one few even though every number is many in itself, as known; so these and their like are states that occur to quantity by comparing some to others. Then 'length' is said by homonymy for meanings: for a single extension however it is, and for the extension that occurs first, and for length of two extensions, and for the single extension from the center of the world to its circumference, and for the height of man which is the dimension containing his first movement. Likewise, 'width' is said for quantity having two dimensions, and for the single dimension that occurs intersecting a dimension supposed first, and for the shorter of two intersecting dimensions, and for the single dimension from right and left. And 'depth' is also said for the thickness enclosed by surfaces, and for the dimension intersecting the two dimensions supposed first, and for what contains the front and back of man, and in other animals its top and bottom. Then the point, if it moves or is supposed to move in some direction, a length and line are depicted; then if this line moves not in the direction of the point's movement but in a direction intersecting it, a surface and width are depicted; then if the surface rises or falls such that its movement is in a dimension intersecting the two dimensions at right angles, a body is depicted, and the apparent of the body insofar as it is its apparent is a simple surface. Then if this surface is cut, the extremity resulting from the cut is the line; and if the line is cut, its extremity is the point. Then the bounded line is the dimension supposed between two points, and the bounded surface is the dimension supposed between two lines, and the bounded depth is the dimension supposed between two surfaces. Then length, width, and depth without relation are quantities, and with relation are accidents.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is a detailed definitional exposition on the category of quantity (al-kamm). The author begins by discussing the nature of continuous quantities that have parts arranged one under another, and how motion does not destroy contiguity but only changes position. He then states that the division of quantity into that with position (dhu wad') and that without position (ghayr dhi wad') is a division by differentiae, implying that these differentiae are essential and not subject to change by accidental motion. He proceeds to list examples: line, surface, body, and place have position for their parts relative to each other or to what contains them. Time, however, lacks position because its parts are not co-existent; though it has order (priority and posteriority), there is no simultaneous juxtaposition. Number has determinacy and order but no continuity, so it lacks position, though it may acquire position accidentally by being associated with things that have position. Speech (al-qawl) is even further from having position because it lacks natural order. The author then clarifies that quantity with position is magnitude (al-miqdar), and there are three primary magnitudes: line, surface, and body; if place is included, they become four. Time, number, and speech are quantities without position—these are the real quantities. He then explains accidental quantities: things that are said to be quantities by accident because they are compared to essential quantities. This includes subjects like man or horse (called tall or short) and accidents like motion (called long or short) or whiteness (called wide). Some accidents are specific to quantity, like relative length or shortness, where lines are called long or short relative to each other even though every line is long in itself. The same applies to surfaces, bodies, and numbers. The author then discusses the term 'length' (tul) and its various meanings by homonymy: a single extension, the first extension, the length of two extensions, the distance from center to circumference, and human height (the dimension of the first movement). Similarly, 'width' (ard) is used for a quantity with two dimensions, for a single dimension intersecting a first dimension, for the shorter of two intersecting dimensions, and for the right-left dimension. 'Depth' (umq) is used for thickness enclosed by surfaces, for the dimension intersecting two dimensions, and for front-back (in humans) or top-bottom (in animals). The passage then explains how geometric entities arise from motion: a moving point generates a line; a moving line (perpendicularly) generates a surface; a moving surface (perpendicularly to both) generates a body. The surface of a body is a simple surface. Cutting a surface yields a line; cutting a line yields a point. A bounded line is a dimension between two points; a bounded surface between two lines; bounded depth between two surfaces. Finally, length, width, and depth without relation (i.e., absolutely) are quantities, but with relation (compared to each other) are accidents.

ذو الوضع

Key Takeaway
The essential division of quantity hinges on whether parts co-exist spatially; line, surface, body, and place have position; time, number, and speech do not.

?

Accidental Quantities: When Things Are Called Quantities by Association
Author explains accidental attribution of quantity to subjects and accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between essential and accidental quantities and identify when a term is applied to a subject or accident because of its relation to an essential quantity.

Core Idea
Many things are called quantities not in themselves, but because they are compared to essential quantities.

Not everything called a quantity is a quantity in the primary sense. The author explains how subjects like man and horse, and accidents like motion and whiteness, are called quantities only accidentally, due to their association with something that truly is a quantity.

Guided Path
1
Understand accidental predication
The author distinguishes between essential quantities (bil-dhat) and accidental quantities (bil-'ard). Accidental quantities are not quantities in themselves but are compared to essential quantities.
This is a key ontological distinction.
2
Identify two sources of accidental quantities
First, subjects that possess a quantity (e.g., man called tall because he has height). Second, accidents that exist in a quantity (e.g., motion called long because it occurs over a distance or time).
The passage gives examples: human and horse for subjects; motion and whiteness for accidents.
3
Recognize relative comparisons specific to quantity
The author notes that even among essential quantities, there are relative accidents like 'long' and 'short' which are comparisons between quantities. Every line is long in itself, but one may be longer than another.
This is a secondary accidental attribution.

Number has determinacy and order but no continuity, so it lacks position, though it may acquire position accidentally by being associated with things that have position. Speech (al-qawl) is even further from having position because it lacks natural order. The author then clarifies that quantity with position is magnitude (al-miqdar), and there are three primary magnitudes: line, surface, and body; if place is included, they become four. Time, number, and speech are quantities without position—these are the real quantities. He then explains accidental quantities: things that are said to be quantities by accident because they are compared to essential quantities. This includes subjects like man or horse (called tall or short) and accidents like motion (called long or short) or whiteness (called wide). Some accidents are specific to quantity, like relative length or shortness, where lines are called long or short relative to each other even though every line is long in itself. The same applies to surfaces, bodies, and numbers. The author then discusses the term 'length' (tul) and its various meanings by homonymy: a single extension, the first extension, the length of two extensions, the distance from center to circumference, and human height (the dimension of the first movement). Similarly, 'width' (ard) is used for a quantity with two dimensions, for a single dimension intersecting a first dimension, for the shorter of two intersecting dimensions, and for the right-left dimension. 'Depth' (umq) is used for thickness enclosed by surfaces, for the dimension intersecting two dimensions, and for front-back (in humans) or top-bottom (in animals). The passage then explains how geometric entities arise from motion: a moving point generates a line; a moving line (perpendicularly) generates a surface; a moving surface (perpendicularly to both) generates a body. The surface of a body is a simple surface. Cutting a surface yields a line; cutting a line yields a point. A bounded line is a dimension between two points; a bounded surface between two lines; bounded depth between two surfaces. Finally, length, width, and depth without relation (i.e., absolutely) are quantities, but with relation (compared to each other) are accidents.

بالعرض

Geometrical Generation: From Point to Body
Author explains how point motion generates dimensions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe how the three dimensions (length, width, depth) arise from the motion of a point and the intersection of dimensions, and define bounded dimensions.

Core Idea
Every dimension is generated by the motion of a lower-dimensional entity.

The author explains a classic thought experiment: a moving point generates a line, a moving line generates a surface, and a moving surface generates a body. This abstraction is key to understanding the structure of physical bodies.

Guided Path
1
Start with a point
A point is dimensionless. When it moves or is imagined to move in a direction, it traces out a line (length).
The point has no position or dimension initially.
2
Move the line perpendicularly
If the line moves not in its own direction but in a perpendicular direction, it traces out a surface (width).
The motion must be intersecting the original direction.
3
Move the surface perpendicularly
If the surface moves in a direction perpendicular to both previous dimensions, it traces out a body (depth).
This gives a solid object.
4
Define bounded dimensions
A bounded line is the dimension between two points. A bounded surface is the dimension between two lines. A bounded depth is the dimension between two surfaces.
These are the limits of geometric entities.

ذا وضع بسبب ما يقارنه. والقول أبعد من ذلك فإنه لا يكون على ترتيب طبيعي. والكم ذو الوضع هو المقدار والمقادير بالحقيقة ثلاثة؛ وإذا أخذ فيها المكان صارت أربعة. فالزمان والعدد والقول غير ذوات وضع؛ فهذه هي الكميات بالحقيقة. وقد يكون من الأشياء ما يقال إنه من الكميات ويكون ذلك بالعرض لا بالذات لما أنه سبب المقارنة لكميات هي بالذات. وذلك إما من الموضوعات لها كالإنسان والفرس فيقال إنه قصير وطويل، وإما من الأعراض التي لا توجد إلا مع الكميات كالحركة فإنها لا توجد إلا بمقارنة من جسم متحرك لما فيه تكون الحركة فيها. والزمان تكون هي أيضًا فيه فتقدر به فيقال حركة طويلة أي في مسافة طويلة أو في زمان طويل، ويقال أيضًا بياض عريض أي في سطح عريض. وقد يكون من هذه العوارض ما يكون خاصة للكمية كالطول والقصر الذي بالقياس مثل ما يقال إن هذا الخط طويل والآخر قصير وإن كان كل خط طويلا في نفسه، وهذا السطح عريض والآخر ضيق وإن كان كل سطح عريضًا في نفسه، وهذا الجسم ثخين والآخر رقيق وإن كان كل جسم ثخينًا في نفسه، وهذا العدد كثير والآخر قليل وإن كان كل عدد كثيرًا في نفسه على ما عرف؛ فهذه وأمثالها أحوال تعرض للكم بمقايسة البعض منها إلى البعض. ثم الطول يقال باشتراك الاسم على معان على امتداد واحد كيف كان وعلى الامتداد الذي يعرض أولاً، وعلى الطول لامتدادين، وعلى الامتداد الواحد من مركز العالم إلى محيطه، وعلى طول الإنسان وهو البعد الذي فيه أول حركته. وكذلك العرض يقال للكم الذي فيه بعدان، وللبعد الواحد الذي يعرض مقاطعًا لبعد فرض أولا؛ ولأقصر البعدين المتقاطعين، وللبعد الواحد من اليمين والشمال والعمق أيضًا يقال للشخن المحصور في السطوح وللبعد المقاطع للبعدين المفروضين أولا ولما يحويه قدام الإنسان وخلفه وفي الغير من الحيوانات أعلاه وأسفله. ثم النقطة إذا تحركت أو فرضت متحركة في جهة ما ارتسم طول و خط؛ فإذا تحرك هذا الخط لا في جهة حركة النقطة بل في جهة مقاطعة لها ارتسم سطح وعرض؛ فإن ارتفع السطح أو انخفض حتى يكون حركة على بعد مقاطع للبعدين على قوائم ارتسم جسم وظاهر للجسم من حيث هو ظاهره فهو بسيط وسطح. فإن قطع هذا السطح فالطرف الحاصل بالقطع فقط هو الخط؛ وإن قطع الخط فطرفه النقطة. ثم الخط المحدود هو البعد الذي يفرض بين نقطتين، والسطح المحدود هو البعد الذي يفرض بين خطين، والعمق المحدود هو البعد الذي يفرض بين سطحين. ثم الطول والعرض والعمق بدون الإضافة من الكميات ومع الإضافة من الأعراض
Supposed to be continuous and some under some, that entails substructure in body. For motion does not remove contiguity between them nor prevent pointing to each two parts one from the other. So that contiguity is preserved even if it changes with external things, but although it is so, it necessitates transfer from position to position, and this does not necessitate that the transferred thing has no position, but rather necessitates that the position changes continuously and discretely. Then if the division of quantity into that with position and that without position is a division by differentiae, it is not permissible that the differentiae be changed by motion and be an accident of body. And it is not hidden that the line has position for its parts, and likewise the surface and the body and place; for their parts have position relative to what is its surface and relative to what encompasses it. As for time, it has no position for its parts, since no part of it exists concomitant with another part, even though there is in its parts continuity and order in terms of priority and posteriority. As for number, even though its parts have determinacy and order, they have no continuity, so it has no position, but it may become possessed of position due to what accompanies it. And speech is further from that, for it is not in a natural order. Quantity with position is magnitude, and magnitudes in reality are three; if place is included in them, they become four. Time, number, and speech are without position; these are the quantities in reality. And there are things that are said to be quantities accidentally, not essentially, because they are the cause of comparison with quantities essentially. That either from the subjects of them, like man and horse, so it is said short and tall, or from accidents that do not exist except with quantities, like motion, for it does not exist except with the comparison of a moving body to what motion occurs in it. And time also exists in it, so it is estimated by it, so it is said long motion, i.e., in a long distance or in a long time, and it is also said wide whiteness, i.e., on a wide surface. And among these accidents there may be what is specific to quantity, like length and shortness relatively, as it is said this line is long and that one is short even though every line is long in itself, and this surface is wide and that one narrow even though every surface is wide in itself, and this body is thick and that one thin even though every body is thick in itself, and this number is many and that one few even though every number is many in itself, as known; so these and their like are states that occur to quantity by comparing some to others. Then 'length' is said by homonymy for meanings: for a single extension however it is, and for the extension that occurs first, and for length of two extensions, and for the single extension from the center of the world to its circumference, and for the height of man which is the dimension containing his first movement. Likewise, 'width' is said for quantity having two dimensions, and for the single dimension that occurs intersecting a dimension supposed first, and for the shorter of two intersecting dimensions, and for the single dimension from right and left. And 'depth' is also said for the thickness enclosed by surfaces, and for the dimension intersecting the two dimensions supposed first, and for what contains the front and back of man, and in other animals its top and bottom. Then the point, if it moves or is supposed to move in some direction, a length and line are depicted; then if this line moves not in the direction of the point's movement but in a direction intersecting it, a surface and width are depicted; then if the surface rises or falls such that its movement is in a dimension intersecting the two dimensions at right angles, a body is depicted, and the apparent of the body insofar as it is its apparent is a simple surface. Then if this surface is cut, the extremity resulting from the cut is the line; and if the line is cut, its extremity is the point. Then the bounded line is the dimension supposed between two points, and the bounded surface is the dimension supposed between two lines, and the bounded depth is the dimension supposed between two surfaces. Then length, width, and depth without relation are quantities, and with relation are accidents.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is a detailed definitional exposition on the category of quantity (al-kamm). The author begins by discussing the nature of continuous quantities that have parts arranged one under another, and how motion does not destroy contiguity but only changes position. He then states that the division of quantity into that with position (dhu wad') and that without position (ghayr dhi wad') is a division by differentiae, implying that these differentiae are essential and not subject to change by accidental motion. He proceeds to list examples: line, surface, body, and place have position for their parts relative to each other or to what contains them. Time, however, lacks position because its parts are not co-existent; though it has order (priority and posteriority), there is no simultaneous juxtaposition. Number has determinacy and order but no continuity, so it lacks position, though it may acquire position accidentally by being associated with things that have position. Speech (al-qawl) is even further from having position because it lacks natural order. The author then clarifies that quantity with position is magnitude (al-miqdar), and there are three primary magnitudes: line, surface, and body; if place is included, they become four. Time, number, and speech are quantities without position—these are the real quantities. He then explains accidental quantities: things that are said to be quantities by accident because they are compared to essential quantities. This includes subjects like man or horse (called tall or short) and accidents like motion (called long or short) or whiteness (called wide). Some accidents are specific to quantity, like relative length or shortness, where lines are called long or short relative to each other even though every line is long in itself. The same applies to surfaces, bodies, and numbers. The author then discusses the term 'length' (tul) and its various meanings by homonymy: a single extension, the first extension, the length of two extensions, the distance from center to circumference, and human height (the dimension of the first movement). Similarly, 'width' (ard) is used for a quantity with two dimensions, for a single dimension intersecting a first dimension, for the shorter of two intersecting dimensions, and for the right-left dimension. 'Depth' (umq) is used for thickness enclosed by surfaces, for the dimension intersecting two dimensions, and for front-back (in humans) or top-bottom (in animals). The passage then explains how geometric entities arise from motion: a moving point generates a line; a moving line (perpendicularly) generates a surface; a moving surface (perpendicularly to both) generates a body. The surface of a body is a simple surface. Cutting a surface yields a line; cutting a line yields a point. A bounded line is a dimension between two points; a bounded surface between two lines; bounded depth between two surfaces. Finally, length, width, and depth without relation (i.e., absolutely) are quantities, but with relation (compared to each other) are accidents.

Relative Nature of Quantitative Attributes
Author explains that length, width, thickness, and number are relative when compared.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a quantitative attribute is relative and explain why it is a state of comparison rather than an absolute property.

Core Idea
Quantity attributes are relative when compared, though absolute in themselves.

In this passage, the author clarifies that terms like 'long' and 'short' are not absolute but depend on comparison. Understanding this distinction is crucial for analyzing propositions in Islamic logic, especially in syllogisms where relative terms appear. Let's break down the examples to see how comparison creates new 'states' (ahwal).

Guided Path
1
Identify the quantitative attributes
Notice the attributes: length (tawl), width ('ard), thickness (thikhana), and number ('adad). Each is a continuous or discrete quantity.
These are all examples of the category of quantity (al-kamm).
2
Understand the relative comparison
For each attribute, the author gives a pair: e.g., 'this line is long, that one is short.' Even though every line is long in itself, the comparison makes one relatively longer.
The phrase 'bi-al-qiyas' means 'by comparison' — not the syllogism but the act of measuring against another.
3
Apply to other examples
Think of other quantitative comparisons in Islamic sciences, like 'this is more intoxicating' or 'this is larger zakat.' Recognize that these involve relative states.
The key is that the attribute itself is absolute, but the comparison generates a new relational state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للكمية كالطول والقصر الذي بالقياس مثل ما يقال إن هذا الخط طويل والآخر قصير وإن كان كل خط طويلا في نفسه، وهذا السطح عريض والآخر ضيق وإن كان كل سطح عريضًا في نفسه، وهذا الجسم ثخين والآخر رقيق وإن كان كل جسم ثخينًا في نفسه، وهذا العدد كثير والآخر قليل وإن كان كل عدد كثيرًا في نفسه على ما عرف ؛ فهذه وأمثالها أحوال تعرض للكم بمقايسة البعض منها إلى البعض.
For quantity, like length and shortness which are by comparison, such as it is said: this line is long and the other is short, even though every line is long in itself, and this surface is wide and the other is narrow, even though every surface is wide in itself, and this body is thick and the other is thin, even though every body is thick in itself, and this number is many and the other is few, even though every number is many in itself, as is known; these and their likes are states that occur to quantity by comparing some of them to others.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage defines the relative nature of certain quantitative attributes. The author gives examples: a line is called 'long' only in comparison to another line that is shorter, even though every line is absolutely long in its own nature. Similarly for width, thickness, and number. The key point is that these attributes are 'states' (ahwal) that arise from comparing one quantity to another, not from absolute existence. This is crucial for understanding how predicates like 'long' function in propositions: they are relative, not absolute, and thus involve a relation (idafa). In Islamic logic, this distinction helps in analyzing syllogisms where such relative terms appear.

Multiple meanings of length and width
Author enumerates the different meanings of the terms length and width.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the distinct technical meanings of 'length' and 'width' as used in classical Islamic geometry and identify the principle of homonymy (ishtirak al-ism).

Core Idea
Length and width are not single concepts—they are homonyms with multiple precise definitions.

In this passage, the author demonstrates the principle of homonymy (ishtirak al-ism) by listing the various technical meanings of the terms 'length' and 'width'. This is not mere lexicography; it is a necessary clarification for understanding geometrical and physical discussions in the Islamic sciences. By parsing these meanings, you will learn how careful terminological precision prevents confusion in arguments about quantity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being discussed
The passage begins with 'length' (al-tawl) and then 'width' (al-'arad). Note that both are said by homonymy (ishtirak al-ism).
2
List the distinct meanings of 'length'
The author provides five meanings: (1) any single extension, (2) the first extension to occur, (3) the length of two extensions, (4) the radius from center to circumference, (5) the length of a human (head-to-toe).
Each meaning has a specific context in geometry or physics.
3
List the distinct meanings of 'width'
For 'width', the author gives four meanings: (1) a quantity with two dimensions, (2) a dimension that intersects a first assumed dimension, (3) the shorter of two intersecting dimensions, (4) the left-to-right dimension.
The list is followed by similar definitions for depth (') albeit with less detail.
4
Understand the principle of homonymy
Recognize that a single word can have multiple technical definitions. This is crucial in Islamic logic to avoid equivocation fallacies.
In later logic, terms like 'length' are often used with a single primary meaning, but here the author shows the full range.

Commentary
This passage defines the various technical meanings of 'length' (tawl) and 'width' ('arad) as used in geometry and physics. Generating Dimensions from Point Movement
Author explains generation of dimensions from point motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace how a point's movement generates line, surface, and body in three perpendicular directions.

Core Idea
Movement of a point in perpendicular steps creates all three spatial dimensions.

In this passage, the author explains a foundational concept in geometric physics: how the three dimensions of length, width, and depth arise from the movement of a dimensionless point. This idea was used by Islamic philosophers to understand the nature of bodies and their boundaries. By studying this generation, you will grasp the logical relationship between points, lines, surfaces, and solids.

Guided Path
1
Visualize the point
The point has no dimension. It is the starting unit. Recognize that any movement from this point will create a trace.
2
Move the point in one direction
Assume the point moves in a single direction. This movement traces a line, which has length (first dimension).
The line is the first extension.
3
Move the line perpendicularly
Take the generated line and move it not in its original direction, but in a direction intersecting it (perpendicular). This trace creates a surface, which has length and width (two dimensions).
4
Move the surface perpendicularly
Finally, move the surface up or down (perpendicular to both previous directions). This trace creates a body, which has length, width, and depth (three dimensions). Notice that the surface of the body is its apparent part, and cutting a body yields a surface, cutting a surface yields a line, cutting a line yields a point.
This also shows that dimensions are reversible: the ends are boundaries of the same type.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم النقطة إذا تحركت أو فرضت متحركة في جهة ما ارتسم طول و خط؛ فإذا تحرك هذا الخط لا في جهة حركة النقطة بل في جهة مقاطعة لها ارتسم سطح وعرض؛ فإن ارتفع السطح أو انخفض حتى يكون حركة على بعد مقاطع للبعدين على قوائم ارتسم جسم وظاهر للجسم من حيث هو ظاهره فهو بسيط وسطح. فإن قطع هذا السطح فالطرف الحاصل بالقطع فقط هو الخط؛ وإن قطع الخط فطرفه النقطة.
Then the point, if it moves or is assumed to move in some direction, a length and a line are traced. Then if this line moves not in the direction of the point's movement but in a direction intersecting it, a surface and a width are traced. Then if the surface rises or lowers such that its movement is in a dimension intersecting the two dimensions at right angles, a body is traced; and the apparent part of the body in terms of its being apparent is simple and a surface. For if this surface is cut, the resulting end from the cut is only a line; and if the line is cut, its end is the point.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage provides a geometric generation of the three dimensions. The author explains that a point moving in one direction traces a line (first dimension). If that line moves perpendicularly (in a direction intersecting the first), it traces a surface (second dimension). If that surface moves perpendicularly (up or down), it traces a body (third dimension). The surface of the body is its apparent outer part. Furthermore, the boundaries: cutting a surface yields a line; cutting a line yields a point. This illustrates the continuous nature of quantity and the derivation of boundaries. It is a standard Euclidean-like account used in Islamic natural philosophy.

Key Terms
النقطة
الخط
السطح
الجسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is generated when a line moves in a direction intersecting its original movement?
A) A point
B) A line
C) A surface
D) A body

Key Takeaway
A point generates a line by moving in one direction; the line generates a surface by moving perpendicularly; the surface generates a body by moving perpendicularly again.

Absolute vs Relative Quantity: The Role of Addition
Author distinguishes absolute from relative quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given term (like 'long', 'many') as belonging to the category of quantity or relation based on the presence or absence of addition (idafa).

Core Idea
Addition transforms quantity into a relation.

In the Aristotelian categories, quantity (al-kamm) and relation (al-idafa) are distinct. This passage teaches us that the same term can shift categories depending on whether it includes an addition. Understanding this distinction is crucial for accurate logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term in context
Read the passage and find terms like 'length', 'many', 'long', 'wide' etc. Note if they are used absolutely or with a comparative sense.
2
Check for 'addition' (idafa)
Determine if the term is used in a way that implies a relation to something else. In Arabic, idafa can be grammatical or logical; here it is logical.
Terms like 'longer' or 'more' contain addition.
3
Apply the rule
If the term is without addition, it belongs to quantity. If with addition, it belongs to accidents (specifically relation).
The author states: 'length without addition is quantity; with addition it is an accident.'
4
Test with examples
Practice classifying: 'depth' (absolute) vs 'deeper' (relative). 'number' (absolute) vs 'greater number' (relative).
Use Islamic examples: 'the length of the prayer' vs 'longer prayer'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الطول والعرض والعمق بدون الإضافة من الكميات ومع الإضافة من الأعراض. وكذلك الكثير بلا إضافة هو العدد وبالإضافة عرض في العدد. ثم الإضافة في الطويل والعريض والعميق والكثير قد تكون على الإطلاق فلا تكون من شرط ما يضاف إليه طرف منها أن يتضمن إضافة إلى ثالث، وقد لا تكون كما في لا أكثر ولا أطول ولا أعمق؛ فإن لكل واحد منها إضافة إلى شيء له إضافة إلى ثالث.
Then length, width, and depth without addition are quantities, and with addition are accidents. And similarly, 'many' without addition is number, and with addition is an accident in number. Then addition in 'long', 'wide', 'deep', and 'many' may be absolute such that it is not a condition of what it is added to that it entails an addition to a third, or it may not be as in 'no more', 'no longer', 'no deeper'; for each of these is an addition to something that has an addition to a third.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage clarifies the distinction between absolute quantity and relative quantity (the category of relation, idafa). The author says that when terms like 'long' are used without relation (idafa), they denote quantity itself; but when used with relation (e.g., 'longer than'), they fall into the category of relation (an accident). Similarly, 'many' without relation is number (a quantity), but 'more' is a relative accident. The author then explains that sometimes the relation is simple (between two things) and sometimes it involves a third term (e.g., 'the longest' implies comparison to a third). This is important for understanding how predicates in propositions can be from different categories, affecting syllogistic reasoning.

Key Terms
كم
إضافة
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When it is used with addition.
B) When it is used without addition.
C) When it is used as a predicate.
D) When it refers to water depth.

Key Takeaway
Addition (idafa) is the marker that distinguishes a quantity from a relational accident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Simple vs Three-Term Relations in Comparative Terms
Author discusses conditions of addition in comparative terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between a simple relation (two-term) and a relation that implies a third term in comparative expressions.

Core Idea
Some comparisons involve a third entity; others are direct between two.

The author extends the analysis of addition by noting that not all relations are alike. Some are absolute, meaning they only require two terms (e.g., 'longer than X'), while others, like 'the longest', entail a relation to a third term. This nuance sharpens logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify comparative terms in the passage
Find terms like 'long', 'wide', 'deep', 'many' used in comparative forms: 'longer', 'more', etc. The passage specifically mentions 'no more', 'no longer', 'no deeper' (لا أكثر, لا أطول, لا أعمق).
These are extreme comparatives.
2
Check if the relation involves a third
For each comparative, consider whether it implies a comparison that could involve more than two items. 'Longer than Z' is two-term; 'longest' implies a third for the comparison group.
The author says 'each of these is an addition to something that has an addition to a third'.
3
Classify the relation type
If the term can be used in a two-term comparison without requiring a third, it is 'absolute addition'. If it structurally implies a third (like superlatives), it is non-absolute.
Use the passage's own examples.
4
Apply to Islamic examples
For instance, in fiqh, 'most preferred action' (afdal) implies a comparison among multiple actions, thus involves a third. 'Better than' may be two-term.

Continuous and Discrete Quantity: Measurement, Counting, and Time
Author defines measurement of continuous and discrete, and discusses the nature of time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between continuous and discrete quantity as differentiae of quantity, and explain the logical status of time and the 'now'.

Core Idea
'Continuous' and 'discrete' are differentiae of quantity, not species.

In this passage, the author clarifies the logical foundations of quantity – one of Aristotle's ten categories. He explains that 'continuous' and 'discrete' are not species (anwa') but differentiae (fusul) that distinguish the species of quantity. He also analyzes time (zaman) and the 'now' (al-an), showing that time is continuous by essence and that the 'now' both separates and connects time like a point in a line. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly defining terms in Islamic logic and for analysing temporal arguments in kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two fundamental operations on quantity
Look for the phrases 'المساحة تقدير المتصل' and 'العد تقدير المنفصل'. Measurement (misaha) estimates continuous quantity; counting ('add) estimates discrete quantity.
2
Distinguish the mental subject from the real object
The measurer and counter are in the soul (النفس); the measured and counted exist in external things. This distinction prevents conflating mental operations with real properties.
3
Understand why counting does not change the nature of a continuous thing
If a measured quantity (e.g., a length) is counted (e.g., 2 meters), the number is accidental; the thing remains continuous. The discrete does not become its genus just because number attaches to it.
4
Analyze the definition of time
Time is defined as the measure of motion (مقدار الحركة). It is continuous by essence (متصل بالذات) but also accidentally estimated by distance (e.g., 'time of a farsakh').
5
Clarify the role of the 'now' (al-an)
The 'now' in time is like a point in a line: it separates the past from the future, but also connects them. Its separative function does not make time discrete; time remains continuous.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والآن نقول إن المساحة تقدير المتصل، والعد تقدير المنفصل، وما هو منهما في النفس فهو العاد والماسح، وما هو منهما في الشيء فهو المعدود والممسوح. فإذا صار الممسوح معدودًا فالعدد عارض له فلا يوجب ذلك أن يصير المنفصل جنسًا له. وإن الزمان متصل بالذات لما أنه في نفسه مقدار الحركة، وبالعرض أيضًا لما أنه يقدر بالمقايسة إلى المسافة فيقال زمان حركة فرسخ فيقدر الزمان بالفرسخ وهذا التقدير له كم الحركة. ثم الشيء يمكن أن يكون في مقولة وقد يعرض له شيء من تلك المقولة: فإن الإضافة يعرض لها الإضافة، والكيفية يعرض لها الكيفية لكنه لا يمكن أن يكون منفصلا إلا بالعرض؛ فإنه يعرض له من الانفصال إلى الساعات والأيام وغير ذلك. ولا يقال إنه منفصل بالذات من حيث هو عدد؛ والآن يوجب فصله. فإن الآن في الزمان معلوم كالنقطة في الخط، ولو كان شيئًا حاصلا لكان فاصلا لكن لا يلزم من كونه فاصلا أن لا يكون واصلا بل هذا الفصل من جملة ما يجتمع مع الوصل؛ وهذا ظاهر. واعلم أن المتصل والمنفصل فصلا الكم لا نوعا الكم إلا وأن تقرن بهما طبيعة الجنس ولكن ليسا من الفصول التي هي غير الأنوع؛ والفصول المنطقية لا تكون غير الأنواع في الموضوع وإلا لا تحمل عليها لكن تكون
And now we say: 'measurement' (al-misaha) is the estimation of the continuous, and 'counting' (al-'add) is the estimation of the discrete. And what is of them in the soul is the counter and the measurer, and what is of them in the thing is the counted and the measured. So if the measured becomes counted, number is accidental to it, and that does not necessitate that the discrete becomes its genus. And time is continuous by essence because it is in itself the measure of motion, and also by accident because it is estimated by comparison to distance, so it is said 'the time of a farsakh's motion', so time is estimated by the farsakh, and this estimation gives it the quantity of motion. Then a thing can be in a category, and something from that category can occur to it: for relation occurs to relation, and quality occurs to quality, but it cannot be discrete except accidentally; for discreteness occurs to it into hours and days and so on. And it is not said that it is discrete by essence in terms of being number; and the 'now' necessitates its separation. For the 'now' in time is known like the point in the line, and if it were something subsisting, it would be a separator, but it does not follow from its being a separator that it is not a connector; rather, this separation is part of what joins with connection; and this is clear. And know that the continuous and the discrete are differentiae of quantity, not species of quantity, unless the nature of the genus is attached to them. But they are not among the differentiae that are other than the species; and logical differentiae are not other than the species in the subject, otherwise they would not be predicated of them, but are...
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of continuous and discrete quantity. The author defines measurement (misaha) as the estimation of continuous quantity, and counting ('add) as the estimation of discrete. The measurer and counter are in the soul; the measured and counted are in the object. If a continuous quantity (e.g., a length) is counted (e.g., as a number of units), the number becomes an accident and does not make the discrete its genus. Time is continuous by essence because it measures motion, but is accidentally estimated by distance (e.g., 'time of a farsakh'). The 'now' (al-an) in time is like a point in a line: it separates but also connects; its separating does not prevent its connecting. Finally, the author clarifies that 'continuous' and 'discrete' are differentiae (fusul) of quantity, not species (anwa'), unless combined with the genus nature. Logical differentiae must be predicated of the species they differentiate.

العد
الزمان
الآن

Key Takeaway
The 'continuous' and 'discrete' are differentiae of quantity, not species; time is continuous by essence and measured by motion.
